Master Thesis
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance
Containership Response Estimation
October, 2025

Supervisor:
Assoc. Prof. Ulrik Dam Nielsen
Co-Supervisor:
PhD., MSc. Raphaël Emile Gilbert Mounet
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
This study investigates the accuracy of model-based predictions of wave-induced ship
motions by combining theoretical response models with reanalysis wave data. Two ap-
proaches were evaluated: a semi-analytical formulation using transfer functions (Case A)
and a numerical approach based on Response Amplitude Operators from a potential-flow
solver (Case B). Both were driven by ERA5 directional wave spectra and validated against
measured response spectra (ground truth) obtained from a containership operating in the
North Atlantic.
The comparison was carried out in both displacement and acceleration domains using
statistical parameters and the Normalised Integrated Absolute Error (NIAE). The results
show that both approaches capture the heave and pitch responses reasonably well, while
roll remains poorly represented due to its strong dependence on viscous and nonlinear
effects. Case B performs slightly better overall, but Case A achieves good agreement de-
spite its simplicity, demonstrating the potential of combining transfer functions with ERA5
data for efficient response estimation.
Preliminary optimisation tests illustrated the sensitivity of the models to key input param-
eters and the trade-offs between different degrees of freedom. The findings highlight the
need for improved damping representation and more advanced optimisation strategies to
enhance the prediction of realistic ship motions.
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation

==PAGE:7==
1
Introduction
Shipping is the backbone of global trade, carrying the majority of goods transported world-
wide. As demands on the industry continue to grow, so does the importance of ensuring
that ships operate efficiently and safely. The performance of a vessel at sea is closely
linked to the surrounding environmental conditions, and in particular to the waves it en-
counters. Understanding the interaction between waves and ship responses is, therefore,
a crucial element in both research and practice.
1.1
Background and Motivation
Ship performance has gained increased importance in recent years. Today’s growing
demand for food, clothing, and other goods worldwide leads to higher shipping volumes.
Shipping companies are naturally concerned with profitability, which they strive to improve
by utilizing cargo space as efficiently as possible. At the same time, fuel consumption opti-
mization has become a central focus. The growing need for vessel performance analysis,
therefore, makes sense, and an increasing number of companies are now dedicating time
to developing tools to assess performance.
For example, shipping companies with established vessel performance departments, such
as Maersk, or specialized companies that focus on vessel performance, provide solutions
that other shipping companies can outsource. For instance, among Danish companies
are Vessel Performance Solutions, Coach Solutions, Danelec, and several others. In ad-
dition, some classification societies such as DNV have introduced digital platforms for
monitoring ship performance. The in-service data analysed in this thesis originates from
DNV.
However, vessel performance is not the only matter of importance. Operational safety
and structural assessment of ships also play key roles. The challenge arises from the
random nature of waves, which cannot be predicted with complete certainty for a specific
time and location. Because of this randomness, the possible way to analyze sea state and
ship responses is through statistical methods. The need to better understand the nature
of waves has motivated extensive research, with numerous studies aimed at enhancing
knowledge. One way to achieve this is through the study of spectral and statistical distri-
butions of sea states (Phelps, 1995). This naturally leads to the question of how loads on
ship are described.
The concept of ship loads can be divided into wave-induced loading on one side and still
water loading on the other. The still water part arises from the distribution of the ship’s
own mass together with cargo, ballast, fuel, and stores, and more broadly it reflects the
balance between mass and buoyancy distributions along the ship’s length (Phelps, 1995).
The more simplified loading approach assumes that the ship is in static equilibrium while
positioned either on the crest or between two crests of an idealized regular wave. These
positions represent extreme wave conditions, and the corresponding wave-induced load
is then superimposed on the still-water load to approximate the total bending moment.
The limitation of this method is that it neglects dynamic effects, the irregular and direc-
tional nature of real seas, and the ship’s motion and structural response, including damp-
ing, bending, and torsion of the hull girder. Therefore, more advanced approaches are
required for a realistic assessment of wave-induced loads (Phelps, 1995).
In contrast to the static approach, dynamic analysis treats the ship as a system respond-
ing to irregular and random waves. This enables the capture of motions in six degrees of
freedom—translation and rotation about the three axes (surge, sway, heave, roll, pitch,
and yaw)—along with structural responses such as bending and torsion. The connection
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
1
==PAGE:8==
between sea state and the ship’s response is commonly described by response ampli-
tude operators (RAOs), also referred to as transfer functions (TRFs). An RAO or TRF
gives a linear relationship between the wave amplitude and the corresponding response
amplitude, and thus between the wave spectrum and the response spectrum. While lin-
ear methods are widely used and practical, they are mainly valid for mild sea conditions
and cannot fully represent effects such as slamming or breaking waves. More advanced
methods are needed for these phenomena (Phelps, 1995); however, the focus of this
thesis is limited to linear approaches.
1.2
Previous work
This section reviews key sources on waves and response spectra, which serve as the
primary references for this project.
Estimation of ship motions using closed-form expressions by J. J. Jensen et al.
(2004)
This paper describes a semi-analytical approach for determining the response
spectra of monohull ships. The closed-form expressions are based on linear theory and
provide a simplified way of estimating ship responses. This work forms the basis for one
of the approaches applied in this thesis, and the example cases presented in the paper
were also useful for validating the implementation of the formulas here. J. J. Jensen et al.
(2004) compared the results from the semi-analytical expressions with those from linear
and nonlinear strip theory, as well as with tank measurements. In contrast, this thesis
compares the results against in-service measurement data. Another difference is that
the present study also compares the semi-analytical approach to RAOs obtained from a
linear panel method, and uses sea state input based on ERA5 reanalysis data rather than
a predefined parametric spectrum.
Wave spectrum estimation conditioned on machine learning-based output using
the wave buoy analogy by Nielsen et al. (2023)
The study by Nielsen et al. (2023),
based on the same in-service data as this thesis, details ship parameters and describes
the on-board measuring system. While introducing the wave buoy analogy and examining
wave integral parameters with error calculations, its primary focus is on wave spectrum
estimation—an inverse problem, as opposed to the response spectra addressed in this
context. Unlike the purely physics-based approach in this thesis, Nielsen et al. (2023) em-
ploys a hybrid method that combines physics-based and machine learning approaches.
1.3
Objectives
The aim of this thesis is to analyse in-service data from a containership in order to evaluate
two different approaches for obtaining response spectra. The measured and processed
time series are used as the reference in this study and will be referred to as the ground
truth, as they represent the closest available description of the actual ship responses to
the encountered sea states. The analysis is based on comparing the two approaches
against this reference by looking at statistical sea state parameters and error measures.
Through this comparison, it becomes possible to see which method gives results that are
closer to reality. The objective is therefore to identify which approach can provide a more
reliable approximation of ship responses, with the purpose of supporting more accurate
estimations when needed.
1.4
Report outline
The first chapter, Introduction, provides background on the studied problem and explains
its motivation. Additionally, it presents related studies and outlines the objectives of this
report.
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
2
==PAGE:9==
Building on the introduction, the second chapter, Theory, outlines the theoretical back-
ground relevant to this study. It also introduces the formulas that form the backbone of
the analysis.
Following the theoretical framework, Chapter 3, Data, details the data used in this study.
With the data established, Chapter 4, Methods, describes the data preprocessing steps
and explains the methodological approach.
The analysis of the obtained results is the focus of Chapter 5, Analysis. Subsequently,
Chapter 6, Discussion, further examines these findings and addresses limitations and
uncertainties.
Finally, Chapter 7, Conclusion, summarizes the work and suggests directions for future
research.

2
Theory
2.1
Linear response framework
The theoretical foundation of this study is linear wave theory, also known as Airy wave
theory. It provides a first-order description of surface gravity waves. This is under the
assumptions of inviscid, incompressible, and irrotational flow. The theory also assumes
small wave steepness1 so that nonlinear interactions can be neglected. Under these as-
sumptions, the fluid motion can be described by a velocity potential that satisfies Laplace’s
equation. This builds on a mass conservation basis. The conservation of momentum is
based on the Bernoulli equation. This is subject to linearised kinematic and dynamic free-
surface and body boundary conditions (Young, 1999).
This framework enables the principle of superposition. At a fixed location, the sea surface
elevation can be expressed as a sum of individual sinusoidal components (Young, 1999):
η(t) =
N
X
i=1
ai sin(ωit + ϕi),
(1)
where ai are the amplitudes, ωi the angular frequencies, and ϕi random phases.
For each wave component the relationship between ω and k is governed by the linear
dispersion relation (Mackay, 2012):
ω2 = gk tanh(kh),
(2)
here, g is the gravitational acceleration and h is the water depth. In deep water (tanh kh →1),
Equation (2) reduces to ω2 = gk.
A realistic sea state is described as a stochastic process. It is expressed through a wave
spectrum Sζ(ω), which represents the distribution of variance (and hence energy) of the
sea surface elevation across frequencies. Extending to two dimensions gives the direc-
tional spectrum:
Sζ(ω, ν) = Sζ(ω)D(ω, ν),
(3)
here, ν is the wave direction and D(ω, ν) is directional spreading function2 (Mackay, 2012).
The one-directional spectrum Sζ(ω) is then defined as (Mackay, 2012)
Sζ(ω) =
Z 2π
0
Sζ(ω, ν)dν
(4)
The spectral description characterises the sea state in terms of energy distribution across
frequencies and directions. This provides the foundation for the next section, which intro-
duces the wave buoy analogy to explain how ship motions relate to the sea state.
2.2
Wave buoy analogy
The fundamental idea of the wave buoy analogy (WBA) is that a vessel’s motions reflect
the sea state it encounters, similar to how a buoy responds to the sea surface elevation.
However, due to its large dimensions and inertia, a ship does not follow the wave surface
completely. Instead, it mainly responds to low- and mid-frequency components, while
high-frequency components are suppressed by inertia and hydrodynamic effects. Thus,
the vessel acts as a high-frequency filter (Nielsen, 2008; Park et al., 2025).
1The wave height is small in comparison to wave length and water depth (Young, 1999).
2It satisfies
∫2π
0
D(ω, ν)dν = 1, and D(ω, ν) ≥0 for [0; 2π] (Mackay, 2012).
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
4
==PAGE:11==
This interpretation explains why ship motions can be linked to the wave spectrum through
transfer functions. Assuming linear time-invariant (LTI) dynamics, the principle of superpo-
sition applies, and the transfer functions remain unchanged for a fixed speed and loading
condition (Nielsen et al., 2023).
Directional estimation requires that at least one measured response vary asymmetrically
between port and starboard; otherwise, waves from opposite directions cannot be dis-
tinguished. Roll naturally provides this asymmetry, but other responses, such as sway
acceleration, may also serve this role (Nielsen et al., 2023).
Building on this theoretical background, the WBA is widely applied to the inverse prob-
lem, where ship responses are used to estimate the incident sea state, for example, in
monitoring and decision-support applications. In the present context, however, the WBA
primarily serves as the theoretical foundation for linking known wave spectra to ship re-
sponse spectra through transfer functions.
2.3
Response spectra
Ship responses in waves are described in terms of response spectra. These are obtained
by combining the directional wave spectrum with the transfer functions (TRFs) of the ves-
sel. For a response R, the encounter–frame spectrum is written as (Nielsen et al., 2025):
Re(ωe, β, U) =
Z 2π
0
H(ωe, β, U)
2 Se(ωe, ν) dν,
(5)
where H(ωe, β, U) is the TRF, and Se(ωe, ν) is the wave spectrum transformed to the
encounter frame.
This transformation between absolute and encounter frequencies is not straightforward,
as the ship’s forward speed alters the apparent frequency of the incident waves. The
phenomenon is known as the Doppler shift, and it determines how the absolute spectrum
S(ω0, ν) maps into the encounter spectrum Se(ωe, ν). The following section develops this
mapping in more detail.
2.4
Doppler shift in response spectra
A vessel moving with forward speed U does not encounter the wave crests at their abso-
lute frequency ω0. Instead, the apparent frequency is shifted according to
ωe =
ω0 −kU cos β
,
(6)
where k is the wave number and β the relative wave heading (β = 180◦head seas, β = 0◦
following seas, β = 90◦beam seas) (Nielsen et al., 2025).
In head seas, the ship meets the waves more often (ωe > ω0). In following seas, the
opposite holds and ωe may even vanish if the ship speed matches the wave celerity. For
beam seas, ωe = ω0.
For head and beam seas (cos β ≤0), the mapping between ω0 and ωe is one-to-one. For
following and stern-quartering seas (cos β > 0), the relation is non-monotonic: up to three
different ω0 values can correspond to the same ωe (Nielsen et al., 2025).

Equation (14) shows that the non-monotonic Doppler shift in following seas causes mul-
tiple absolute frequency branches to contribute to the same encounter frequency. This
mapping conserves spectral energy and forms the basis for consistent response spec-
trum calculations in the encounter frame. The next section introduces one approach for
obtaining transfer functions: the semi-analytical method of J. J. Jensen et al. (2004).
2.5
Semi-analytical transfer functions (TRFs) for vertical motions and roll
A simplified semi-analytical formulation for ship motions was proposed in J. J. Jensen et
al. (2004). The model requires only the main particulars — length L, breadth B, draught
T, block coefficient Cb, waterplane area coefficient CWP — together with the operational
profile (speed U and heading β). It is based on the assumptions of a homogeneously
loaded, box-shaped vessel and neglects coupling between different motions. To make the
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
6
==PAGE:13==
formulation better suited for practical ship geometries, J. J. Jensen et al. (2004) introduced
empirical corrections. For example, the breadth is replaced with B = B0Cb, where B0 is
the maximum waterline breadth and Cb is the block coefficient. The following expressions
define the heave, pitch, and roll motion transfer functions (TRFs).
2.5.1
Heave and pitch transfer functions
For regular incident waves of amplitude a, the uncoupled equations of motion for heave
w and pitch θ can be written as (J. Jensen, 2001):

roll is overestimated close to resonance.
2.6
Linear Rankine panel method
The Rankine panel method is a boundary element technique for modeling potential flow
around floating bodies. In this thesis, only the basic concept is introduced to give context,
while more complete descriptions can be found in the literature by Hess and Smith (1967),
and Bertram (2012). The flow is assumed inviscid, incompressible, and irrotational, lead-
ing to Laplace’s equation for the velocity potential. The wetted hull surface is divided into
panels, each assigned a Rankine source.
A Rankine source is the fundamental solution of Laplace’s equation in free space, repre-
senting a point fluid source. Its potential is given by
ϕ(r) =
1
4πr,
(37)
where r = |x −x0| is the distance between the field point x and the source point x0 (Hess
& Smith, 1967). The velocity field is obtained as the gradient of the potential,
⃗v = ∇ϕ,
(38)
which directly follows from potential flow theory (Bertram, 2012).
These sources are distributed over the hull surface, and their strengths are solved such
that boundary conditions are satisfied: no flux through the hull, free-surface, seabed, and
radiation conditions at infinity. The resulting linear system yields the velocity potentials
for diffraction and radiation analysis, from which added mass, damping coefficients, and
wave excitation forces are derived in the frequency domain (Bertram, 2012; Hess & Smith,
1967).
4Wave length λ, defined as λ = 2π/k

==PAGE:17==
2.7
Fast Fourier Transform (FFT)
The Fourier transform provides a way to represent a time-domain signal as a superposition
of sinusoidal components. For a continuous signal x(t), the Fourier transform is defined
as (Brigham, 1988):
X(ω) =
Z ∞
−∞
x(t)e−iωt dt,
(39)
with inverse
x(t) = 1
2π
Z ∞
−∞
X(ω)eiωt dω.
(40)
When a signal is sampled in time, the discrete Fourier transform (DFT) expresses a finite
sequence of N samples x[n] as a linear combination of complex exponentials, yielding its
frequency-domain representation X[k] (Oppenheim & Schafer, 2014):
X[k] =
N−1
X
n=0
x[n]e−i2πkn/N,
k = 0, 1, . . . , N −1,

with the inverse transform
x[n] = 1

k=0
X[k]ei2πkn/N.
(42)
The DFT provides an exact relation between the time-domain sequence and its frequency
components. However, its direct computation requires O(N2) operations.
The fast Fourier transform (FFT) is an efficient algorithm that computes the DFT using only
O(N log N) operations (Cooley & Tukey, 1965). This efficiency enables practical spectral
analysis of large time series and forms the basis of modern digital signal processing.
In the present context, FFT-based methods are applied to transform measured time series
oboard the ship into the frequency domain, where cross-spectral densities (CSDs) and
response spectra can be estimated.
2.8
Welch’s method
A single periodogram of a time series often gives a noisy spectrum. Welch’s method
reduces variance by splitting the record into overlapping segments, windowing each seg-
ment, forming a periodogram, and averaging the results (Smith, 2011; Welch, 1967).
Define windowed frames
xm[n] = w[n] x[n + mR],
n = 0, . . . , M −1, m = 0, . . . , K −1,
(43)

2.9
Spectral statistics
A spectrum S(ω) can be approximately described by a set of integral wave parameters,
that are commonly defined through spectral moments
mn =
Z ∞
0
ωnS(ω) dω,
(46)
where n denotes the order of the moment, and the zeroth-order moment m0 corresponds
to the variance of the spectra (Lewis, 1989; Mackay, 2012).
Significant wave height Hs.
The significant wave height is defined by
Hs = 4√m0.
(47)
It quantifies the overall energy level — through the zeroth spectral moment m0 — in phys-
ically interpretable units of wave height. Assuming Gaussian seas, the spectral definition
Hs = 4√m0 provides an estimate of H1/3, the mean height of the highest third of waves
(Faltinsen, 1990; Mackay, 2012), with the average wave amplitude given by A = Hs/2.
Zero-crossing period Tz.
The mean zero-crossing period is defined as
Tz = 2π
rm0
m2
.
(48)
It corresponds to the average interval between successive upward crossings of the mean
level (Lewis, 1989; Mackay, 2012). Because m2 weights higher frequencies more strongly
through f2, Tz penalizes high-frequency components (shorter waves) and may exagger-
ate their influence.
Peak period Tp.
Tp is defined from the spectral peak frequency ωp by
Tp = 2π
ωp
.
(49)
It indicates the period that corresponds to the maximum value of S(ω). Tp is sensitive to
spectral shape and to numerical smoothing (Faltinsen, 1990; Mackay, 2012).
Mean period Tm.
The mean wave period is defined as
Tm01 = 2πm0
m1
.
(50)
This represents a weighted average period that reflects how energy is distributed across
the spectrum. It can be understood as the average contribution of the spectral frequency
components (Lewis, 1989; Mackay, 2012).
The parameters Hs, Tz, Tp, and Tm01 are short-term statistical measures: they describe
a stationary, ergodic sea state, meaning that conditions are assumed unchanged during
the interval, while the record is long enough to be representative of the sea state. In
practice, windows of about 20–30 minutes are commonly used, although longer intervals
are also possible — Dietz (2005) suggests up to 3 hours, while Faltinsen (1990) mentions
durations up to 10 hours.
Long-term statistics, by contrast, are obtained by combining many short-term states with
their probabilities of occurrence, leading to overall distributions, scatter diagrams, or esti-
mates of extreme conditions (Faltinsen, 1990; Lewis, 1989). This, however, is not within
the scope of this thesis.
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
12
==PAGE:19==
3
Data
This section introduces the data used in the project, covering both ship measurements
and wave spectra obtained from ERA5.
3.1
ERA5 wave data
Directional wave spectra were obtained from ERA5, the latest global atmospheric and
wave reanalysis produced by the European Centre for Medium-Range Weather Fore-
casts (ECMWF) (Copernicus Climate Change Service (C3S) Climate Data Store (CDS).,
2023; Hersbach et al., 2023). ERA5 uses a weather model together with observations,
combining them through data assimilation to provide a consistent description of the atmo-
sphere, ocean waves, and land surface. It provides hourly coverage on a global grid with
a spacing of 0.5° × 0.5° for wave fields (ECMWF, 2025).
The wave component of ERA5 is based on the third-generation spectral wave model
WAM. This model describes the evolution of the two-dimensional wave energy spectrum
by accounting for wind input, nonlinear wave–wave interactions, and dissipation. The
spectra are discretised into 30 frequencies, starting at 0.0345 [Hz] with geometric spac-
ing, and 24 directions with an angular step of 15° (ECMWF, 2025). The wave directions
in ERA5 are defined relative to true north, with 0° corresponding to north and angles in-
creasing clockwise. They are given in the oceanographic to convention, meaning that the
values indicate the direction the waves are travelling towards.
For this study, spectra were downloaded for each month of the 19-month monitoring period
and extracted at hourly intervals for all grid cells covering the vessel’s full operating area
under deep-water conditions.
Although ERA5 is widely used in both research and industry, providing a long, consistent
record that extends back to 1940, it is not based solely on observations but rather on model
results constrained by them. Biases are therefore possible, especially during extreme
storms or in regions with sparse observational input (ECMWF, 2025). Nevertheless, ERA5
remains one of the most reliable and accessible datasets for wave studies.
An example of an ERA5 directional spectrum is shown in Figure 1.

3.2
Vessel
The analysis is based on recordings from a single vessel, a Panamax container ship with
a capacity of 2800 [TEU], built in 1998. Its main particulars are listed in Table 1.
Parameter
Symbol
Value
Unit
Length between perpendiculars
Lpp
232.0
[m]

Table 1: Ship’s main particulars (Nielsen et al., 2024)
Full-scale measurements were conducted over a four-year period, from August 2007
to July 2011. The data available for this project, however, cover only 19 months, from
September 2007 to March 2009. This limitation originated from the fact that the vessel’s
wave radar (Wavex® by Miros), which provided directional wave information, was opera-
tional only during this interval (Nielsen et al., 2023). Although the present work does not
make use of the Wavex® radar records, the data shared for analysis were restricted to
the same 19-month period.
During this time, the vessel was engaged in transatlantic service between Western Europe
and Quebec, Canada. To avoid shallow-water and coastal effects, the dataset was further
limited to deep-water conditions by applying spatial boundaries at longitudes -55° and -
5°, following the criteria reported in Nielsen et al. (2023). As the provided data extended
beyond this range, the restriction was implemented during the preprocessing stage.
3.3
Onboard measurement system
The measurement setup onboard the vessel is described in detail by Storhaug et al.
(2007). For the present study, the relevant signals are the ship motions together with
basic navigational data.
The main motion sensor was a Motion Response Unit (MRU), installed near the ship’s
center of gravity (COG). More specifically, it was located 78.5 [m] forward of the aft per-
pendicular, 11.7 [m] above the baseline, and on the centreline. The MRU measured all
six degrees of freedom (DOF): surge, sway, heave, roll, pitch, and yaw. In the present
analysis, only heave, pitch, and roll are considered, where heave represents a transla-
tional motion and pitch and roll are angular motions. The unit was mounted in a socket
that damped vibrations in the frequency range of 50–100 [Hz] caused by machinery such
as thrusters and pumps. Since the analysis focuses on wave-frequency motions (periods
of several seconds), this filtering has no influence on the signals of interest and mainly
serves to improve the quality of the recorded data.
Navigational information, including GPS position, Speed Over Ground (SOG), and head-
ing, was logged continuously throughout the measurement period.
Several additional sensors were also part of the monitoring system, although their data
are not used in the present work. These included a vertical accelerometer at the forward
perpendicular, strain gauges at aft and midship sections (converted into bending moment
and axial stress signals), and a Miros Wavex® radar for directional wave spectra.
The dataset does not include measurements of draught or trim. The study by Storhaug
et al. (2007) suggests that typical transit values lie around 9.5 [m] for draught and 0.5–
1.0 [m] for trim. Information on the loading condition was also missing, which means the
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
14
==PAGE:21==
transverse metacentric height GMT could not be specified. These omissions introduce
additional uncertainty, particularly for roll motions, as the transfer functions were computed
for a single draught only.
3.4
Navigational data
For the present work, a navigational dataset extracted from the onboard measurement
system was provided. It consists of 7,644 entries collected between September 2007 and
March 2009. The data were recorded in 25-minute periods, followed by a short pause
for processing and logging, resulting in half-hourly entries in the dataset. Each record
contains a time stamp (dd-MMM-yyyy HH:mm:ss), ship position, speed, and heading. The
position is given in longitude and latitude [deg], where both uncorrected and corrected
values were available. Since the raw coordinates contained errors, only the corrected
positions are used in this analysis. The ship speed variable was stored as Uvec in the
files; in this thesis it is denoted consistently as U. It is reported in [knots] as the average
over the 25-minute measuring period, and the heading is given in degrees relative to true
north.
3.5
Transfer functions
The provided transfer functions (RAOs) consisted of five datasets. Each was computed
with DNV’s Wasim software. Wasim is a hydrodynamic analysis tool that can be applied
to both ships and fixed structures. It handles cases with or without forward speed and
calculates global ship motions and local pressure loads. It solves the three-dimensional
radiation–diffraction problem by a Rankine panel method in the time domain, and the
results can be expressed in the frequency domain. Wasim is capable of both linear and
nonlinear analysis (DNV, n.d.), but the transfer functions provided here are the result of
linear analysis.
Each dataset contains transfer functions as real and imaginary parts over frequency [Hz]
for the six DOF (surge, sway, heave, roll, pitch, yaw) and additional structural responses.
The encounter heading is given from 0° to 350° in steps of 10°, where 0° corresponds to
head seas and 180° to following seas.5 In the present work, only heave, pitch, and roll
are considered, with transfer function units of [m/m] for heave and [deg/m] for pitch and
roll.
The transfer functions were calculated for a single transit draught of 9.5 [m]. This in-
troduces a limitation, since variations in draught during service are not represented. In
addition, they are available only for a limited number of discrete speeds, which restricts
their ability to reflect the full range of operating conditions. While the heading resolution
can be addressed by interpolation, the restriction to 5 discrete speeds (17-21 [knots])
introduces some error.
3.6
Processed response time series
Processed response time series were provided in the form of 7,644 records, matching
the number of entries in the navigational dataset. Each record corresponds to a 25-
minute measurement period with a sampling interval of ∆t=0.2 [s], corresponding to fre-
quency resolution of 5 [Hz]. The files contain roll acceleration [deg/s2], pitch acceleration
[deg/s2], sway acceleration [m/s2], heave acceleration [m/s2], vertical acceleration at the
bow [m/s2], and vertical bending moment amidships [GNm]. In the present work, only
heave, pitch, and roll responses are used.
5This convention follows the Wasim output format; it differs from the sign convention used in the theoretical
framework of Section 2.1.
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
15
==PAGE:22==
These response measurements are treated as the reference dataset, hereafter referred
to as the ground truth, and they form the basis for evaluating the accuracy of the response
spectra estimation methods presented later.

4
Methods
This section describes the procedures used to prepare the datasets and to compute and
evaluate ship responses.
4.1
Preprocessing
The following outlines the preprocessing applied to ERA5 spectra, navigational data,
RAOs, and response time series to prepare them for the analysis.
4.1.1
Preprocessing of navigational data
The ship’s transatlantic routes included both open-ocean passages and coastal segments.
Since the present study focuses on deep-water conditions, the dataset was restricted by
applying longitude limits at -55° and -5°, following previous studies such as Nielsen et
al. (2023). All entries outside this interval were removed. Figure 2 illustrates the vessel
routes over the monitoring period, together with the applied boundary lines. After this
filtering step, the dataset was reduced from 7,644 to 5,261 records.
70°W
60°W
50°W
40°W
30°W
20°W
10°W
0°
10°E
35°N
40°N
45°N
50°N
55°N
Longitude limits (-55°, -5°)
Figure 2: Ship tracks recorded during the monitoring period in the North Atlantic. Vertical
lines mark the longitude limits at -55° and -5°, which were applied to restrict the dataset
to deep-water conditions. Gaps in the tracks reflect missing entries in the navigational
dataset rather than discontinuities in the voyages.
Moreover, the transfer functions described in Section 3.5 were available only for five dis-
crete forward speeds (17–21 [knots]). To match these, the ship’s speed records were first
filtered: samples with speeds outside the interval 16.5–21.4 [knots] were removed. The
remaining speeds were then rounded half up to the nearest integer, so that each sample
corresponds to one of the five transfer function speeds. This filtering reduced the dataset
to 4,903 valid records, as illustrated in Figure 3.
However, rounding introduces some error, as there is a mismatch between the measured
and available speeds. To assess this effect, a supplementary study was carried out in
which transfer functions (following the theory by J. J. Jensen et al. (2004)) were com-
puted for both the actual recorded speeds and the corresponding rounded speeds, across
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
17
==PAGE:24==
0
5
10
15
20
25
Ship Speed [knots]
0
250
500
750
1000
1250
1500
1750
2000
No. samples
Figure 3: Distribution of ship speed. Solid red lines at 17 and 21 [knots] indicate the
range of discrete speeds used, and dashed lines at 16.5 and 21.4 [knots] mark the filtering
thresholds.
headings from 0° to 350° in 10° steps. The difference between the two sets was quantified
using the root-mean-square error (RMSE). On average, the discrepancies remained be-
low 1% for heave and around 2% for pitch and roll (see Appendix 2). Since the response
spectra are proportional to |H(ω)|2, the relative errors in the spectra are expected to be at
most about twice those reported for the transfer functions. Even under this conservative
assumption, the influence of speed rounding can be considered negligible, and round-
ing speeds to the nearest integer [knot] is considered acceptable for the purposes of this
study.
J. J. Jensen et al. (2004) present results for a Panamax containership with L = 270 [m],
considering forward speeds in the range Fn = 0.22–0.27 (approximately 22–27 [knots]).
Referring to the findings of Flokstra (1974), the study notes that the ship motion responses
depend only very slightly on speed within this interval. Although the present vessel is
somewhat shorter and operated mainly between 17–21 [knots], this supports the as-
sumption that speed has only a minor influence on the transfer functions, and thus on
the response spectra, in the current study.
Further details and figures are provided in Appendix 2.
4.1.2
Preprocessing of RAO files
For the present study, only heave, roll, and pitch RAOs were considered. The original files
contained 36 heading blocks, covering the relative heading between 0°–350° in steps of
10°, with each block sharing the same frequency discretisation. During preprocessing, the
files were reorganised so that the RAOs form two-dimensional arrays on a common (f, β)
grid, with one axis representing frequency and the other representing heading. Roll and
pitch RAOs were converted from their original units [deg/m] to [rad/m], while heave RAOs
were kept in [m/m]. This restructuring ensured that the RAOs could be accessed directly
as complex-valued functions of frequency and heading in the subsequent analysis.
4.1.3
Preprocessing of time series
When samples were removed from the navigational dataset, the matching time-series
records were also discarded. This ensured that the time series remained aligned with the
valid navigational data throughout the analysis.
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
18
==PAGE:25==
4.1.4
Preprocessing of ERA5 directional wave spectra
General ERA5 characteristics are described in Section 3.1; here only preprocessing steps
are outlined.
ERA5 provides two-dimensional wave spectra on a fixed grid with 30 frequency bins and
24 directional bins. The frequencies are geometrically spaced starting at 0.03453 [Hz],
and the directions start at 7.5° with a step of 15°, covering the range 7.5°–352.5°. As
part of the decoding, missing values flagged by ERA5 were replaced with zero, and the
spectra, which are stored as base-10 logarithms of energy density, were converted back
to linear form with units of [m2s rad−1] (ECMWF, 2025). An additional directional bin was
then added at (7.5+360)° to close the circle and ensure wrap-around. The directions are
given in the to convention (see Section 3.1). To align them with the transfer functions and
theoretical framework, which use the from convention, the spectra were converted once
during preprocessing. All further analysis in this thesis is therefore carried out in the from
convention.
The reanalysed spectra are provided on a 0.5° × 0.5° latitude–longitude grid. For each
ship record, the four nearest grid nodes (two in latitude × two in longitude) were identi-
fied. The geodesic distance between the ship’s corrected position and each candidate
node was computed in kilometres using the geopy.distance.geodesic6 function, which
accounts for the ellipsoidal shape of the Earth. The ERA5 spectrum at the closest node
(minimum geodesic distance) was then selected. The distribution of the distances is pre-
sented in Figure 4, and a visual representation of all ship locations and assigned ERA5
points is shown in Figure A.3.
As mentioned, the recordings have a duration of 30 minutes, whereas ERA5 spectra are
available only at hourly resolution. For each navigational record, a matching spectrum
from the previously selected grid cell was therefore needed. When the record aligned
with a full hour, the corresponding ERA5 spectrum was used directly. For mid-hour cases
(e.g., 12:30), the spectrum was obtained by linear interpolation between the ERA5 spectra
defined at the two closest hourly timestamps.
Implementation note: At first, the spectra were interpolated in space, time, frequency,
and direction all at once before picking the nearest grid node. This turned out to be
computationally heavy and not practical for larger datasets. The procedure was therefore
changed: first the nearest grid point was found using geodesic distance, and then, for
records falling between two full-hour timestamps, interpolation was done only in time.
With this change, the runtime dropped from over 15 hours without finishing to just a few
minutes on the same dataset.
4.2
Time series spectral estimation
The ship response time series (Section 3.6) were transformed into encounter–frame fre-
quency spectra using the cross-spectral density (CSD) function scipy.signal.csd7. For
each 25-minute record, heave acceleration [m/s2], pitch acceleration [deg/s2], and roll ac-
celeration [deg/s2] were analysed, with angular accelerations converted to radians prior
to spectral estimation. All spectra were estimated as one-sided power spectral densities
(per [Hz]).
The CSD function was applied with sampling frequency fs = 5 Hz (time step ∆t = 0.2 s),
segment length nperseg = 512, noverlap = 256 (50% overlap), and Hann windowing.
6Documentation: https://geopy.readthedocs.io/en/stable/#geopy.distance.geodesic
7Documentation: https://docs.scipy.org/doc/scipy/reference/generated/scipy.signal.csd.html
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
19
==PAGE:26==
Figure 4: Distribution of distances [km] between ship positions and their assigned ERA5
grid points. The plot also indicates the mean value, 95th percentile, and maximum dis-
tance.
With this configuration the Nyquist limit is
fNyq = 1
2fs = 2.5 Hz,
which defines the maximum resolvable frequency in the spectra. The frequency resolution
is ∆f = fs/nperseg ≈0.0098 [Hz]. Larger segment lengths were tested but produced
noisier spectra and greater discrepancies compared with model-based spectra; nperseg
= 512 was therefore selected as a compromise between smoothness and resolution.
Based on inspection of the resulting spectra, a cut-off frequency of 0.3 Hz in encounter
frequency was applied. Above this limit, most spectra contain negligible energy. It is
stressed that this cut-off was chosen as a pragmatic modelling assumption, not estab-
lished through a full inspection of all spectra.
Common frequency grids
To ensure consistency across all analysis steps, both the absolute-frequency wave spec-
tra and the encounter-frequency response spectra were placed on uniformly spaced grids
with a resolution of 0.001 [Hz]. The ERA5 wave spectra S(f0, ν) were linearly interpolated
to a uniform f0 grid, while the encounter grid fe was defined on [0, 0.3] [Hz] with ∆fe =
0.001 [Hz]. The ground truth spectra obtained from csd (native spacing ∆f ≈0.0098 [Hz]
for nperseg = 512) were likewise linearly interpolated onto this common grid. This resam-
pling does not increase the actual spectral resolution; it only ensures that all spectra share
the same frequency basis for integration, comparison, and the extraction of statistical pa-
rameters. The finer spacing was applied uniformly across all cases to capture the spectral
shape in sufficient detail and to make subsequent calculations directly comparable.
4.3
Response spectra comparison domains
The onboard measurement system provided response signals directly in terms of acceler-
ations, which were used to form the ground truth (GT) spectra (Section 3.6). By contrast,
Case A (semi-analytical transfer functions) and Case B (provided RAOs) naturally produce
response spectra at the motion level, i.e. in terms of displacements or angles (heave [m],
pitch and roll [rad]). This mismatch lead to two consistent comparison domains:
• Displacement-based analysis: The GT acceleration spectra, with units [(m/s2)2/Hz]
for heave, and [(rad/s2)2/Hz] for pitch and roll, were converted to motion spectra
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
20
==PAGE:27==
using
Sηη(fe) = Saa(fe)
(2πfe)4 ,
giving [(m)2/Hz] for heave and [(rad)2/Hz] for pitch and roll. This ensured that all
spectra (GT, Case A, Case B) were expressed at the motion level.
• Acceleration-based analysis: Alternatively, the displacement spectra from Case A
and Case B were converted into acceleration spectra,
Saa(fe) = (2πfe)4 Sηη(fe) ,
giving [(m/s2)2/Hz] for heave and [(rad/s2)2/Hz] for pitch and roll. In this way, the GT
remained unchanged, and all spectra were compared directly at the acceleration
level.
The result is two consistent sets of statistics: one based on motions and one based on
accelerations. Transfer functions are usually discussed at the motion level because it is
easier to interpret physical responses. However, using the acceleration domain allows
a direct comparison with the onboard measurements, which were recorded as accelera-
tions, and therefore remain closest to the GT.
4.4
Outlier detection
From each of the three DOFs ground truth response spectra (heave, pitch, and roll), and
in both analysis domains (acceleration and displacement), the response amplitude Hm0/2
and the peak period Tp were extracted. Inspection of their distribution helped to determine
pronounced outliers. Some were clearly non-physical (e.g. displacement amplitudes of
232 [m] in heave), while others were physically possible but lay well outside the clus-
tered values. Outliers were therefore identified based on visual inspection of histograms
rather than a formal statistical outlier-detection formula. Appendix 4 provides illustrative
histograms and corresponding data tables.
Because outlier detection depends on the spectral domain, different limits were applied
in the displacement-based and acceleration-based analysis. Table 2 summarizes the
thresholds used.
Table 2: Outlier filtering thresholds in the two analysis domains.
Acceleration domain
Displacement domain
Peak period Tp
5–12 s
≤26 s
Heave Hm0/2
≤2.0 m/s2
≤2.0 m
Pitch Hm0/2
–
≤0.05 rad
Roll Hm0/2
–
≤0.20 rad
As can be seen, fewer limits were set in the acceleration domain, since pitch and roll
amplitudes did not exhibit pronounced outliers there or they were contained by removing
samples base on the Tp and Hm0/2 limits. By contrast, in the displacement domain ad-
ditional limits were necessary to exclude extreme pitch and roll responses arising after
conversion from accelerations.
Note that to determine the peak period Tp, a code developed by Mounet and Nielsen
(2025) was applied. This approach is based on the Python polyfit function, which fits
a polynomial around the spectral peak to estimate the maximum more accurately. Ini-
tially, Tp was obtained by simply identifying the frequency corresponding to the maximum
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
21
==PAGE:28==
spectral density (using argmax). However, this procedure strongly depends on the dis-
crete frequency resolution of the spectrum, and so several realizations would fall onto
the same frequency grid point, leading to scatter plots with distinct vertical or horizontal
banding. By contrast, the polynomial fitting method provides a smoother representation
of the spectral peak, reducing the sensitivity to frequency discretization and yielding more
consistent estimates of Tp. This method was therefore applied throughout the entire anal-
ysis.
4.5
Case A: using semi-analytical TRFs
Case A reconstructs encounter–frame response spectra from directional wave spectra
by applying the transfer functions based on theory by J. J. Jensen et al. (2004) together
with the encounter mapping. The required inputs are the matched ERA5 spectra on a
frequency–direction grid, ship speed U, relative heading β, and the hydrostatic parame-
ters (L, B, T, Cb, CWP , GMT , ρ) with tuning coefficients (δ, µ).
The two–dimensional spectrum S(f0, ν) was taken from the matched ERA5 product (Sec-
tion 3.1). The frequency axis f0 is in [Hz] and the wave direction ν in degrees using the
from convention. Directions were sorted in ascending order and the duplicate wrap at
0°/360° was removed. The spectra have units of [m2·s·rad−1], which is the format ex-
pected by the response routine.
Since ERA5 frequencies are geometrically spaced, the spectra were interpolated onto a
uniformly spaced grid covering the same range. The frequencies were kept in ascending
order, as required for the encounter mapping to remain valid (Nielsen et al., 2025).
Relative headings β were computed as in Section 2.5, wrapped to [0°,360°). Transfer
functions for heave, pitch, and roll were then evaluated on the (f0, β) grid using the vessel
particulars in Table 1, with GMT = 1.15 [m], δ = 0.62 [-], µ = 0.4 [-], ρ = 1025 [kg/m3], and
g = 9.81 [m/s2]. To avoid problems at the edges of the heading range, the first (0°) and
last (350°) columns were copied and placed just outside the domain, at −ε and 360° + ε,
where ε = 1×10−6. This small buffer ensures that the interpolation does not fail at the
boundaries.
The encounter–frame response spectrum was computed using the resp_spec_2d Python
function (Mounet & Nielsen, 2025), which applies the Doppler mapping between absolute
and encounter frequencies. Its required inputs are the absolute frequencies f0, the corre-
sponding wave spectrum S(f0), the transfer functions H(f0, β), the encounter–frequency
vector fe, ship speed U, and relative heading β. For comparability with the ground truth,
the same encounter–frequency grid fe was used. The function returns displacement auto-
spectra Sηη(fe), and their conversion to acceleration spectra was handled as part of the
comparison framework described in Section 4.3. A uniform cut-off at 0.3 [Hz] was applied.
The record set was restricted using the identified outliers from the ground truth spectra
(Section 4.4), ensuring that Case A is compared one-to-one with the same physically
consistent samples.
Implementation note: The parameter δ was not specified and was therefore determined
such that the ratio B1/T remains within the recommended range 1 ≤B1/T ≤3. Since δ is
constrained to values between 0 and 1, the largest admissible value satisfying B1/T ≥1,
was found to be δ = 0.62 [–]. This value is adopted in the present analysis. For further
context, see Section 2.5.2. Moreover, the parameter GMT was not provided, and the
value of 1.15 [m] used in this work is based on what J. J. Jensen et al. (2004) used for the
Panamax vessel examined in his work. Note that the ship’s dimensions differ to the ones
used in this thesis.
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
22
==PAGE:29==
4.6
Case B: using RAOs
Case B reconstructs the encounter–frame response spectra using precomputed complex
RAOs from a linear Rankine panel method (provided by DNV). Unlike in Case A, no ana-
lytical formulas were used here. The RAOs were applied directly after the preprocessing
described in Section 4.1, where the files were reorganised, converted, and interpolated
onto a common (f0, β) grid for heave, pitch, and roll.
For each navigational record, the matched ERA5 directional spectrum S(f0, ν)—selected
according to the ship’s position and timestamp—was combined with the RAO dataset
corresponding to the nearest available forward speed. This rounding procedure, filtering,
and its associated error estimation are described in Section 4.1. In short, each record
was linked to one of the discrete RAO speeds (17–21 [knots]), ensuring that the ERA5
spectrum and the RAOs represented the same operational condition.
The combined inputs were then used to compute the encounter–frame response spectra
through the routine resp_spec_2d (Mounet & Nielsen, 2025), using the same encounter–
frequency grid and cut-off settings as in Case A. The outputs are displacement spectra,
which were subsequently converted according to the comparison domains in Section 4.3.
To enable one-to-one comparison across all approaches, the same outlier masks derived
from the ground truth spectra (Section 4.4) were applied.
See Figure 5 that summarises the overall workflow used in this thesis.
4.7
Evaluation metrics for spectral comparison
The accuracy of the estimated spectra relative to the reference was assessed using four
common error metrics. Let S∗(ω) denote the estimated spectrum and S(ω) the reference.
Root Mean Square Error (RMSE).
RMSE =

RMSE is the quadratic mean of pointwise differences. Because deviations are squared
before averaging, the metric emphasises large errors, which is useful for detecting mis-
matches near spectral peaks, but it can also overweight outliers (Willmott & Matsuura,
2005).
Mean Absolute Error (MAE).
MAE =

(52)
MAE measures the average absolute deviation between two spectra. Unlike RMSE, all
errors contribute in proportion to their size, making MAE easier to interpret as the typical
vertical separation between the curves (Willmott & Matsuura, 2005).

NIAE represents the fraction of total spectral energy that is misrepresented. It integrates
the absolute deviation over all frequencies and normalises by the reference zeroth mo-
ment m0,ref =
R
S(ω) dω. The metric is dimensionless, with NIAE = 0 indicating perfect
agreement (Nielsen et al., 2024, 2025).
Quantiles of the error distribution.
In addition to the global metrics, quantiles of the
pointwise absolute error
S∗(ωi) −S(ωi)
 were used to describe the distribution of devia-
tions. Reported values include the median (50%) and the 90th percentile, which highlight
both typical discrepancies and less frequent, larger errors (NIST - National Institute of
Standards and Technology, n.d.). These were calculated using NumPy’s quantile()
function, which determines the value below which a given percentage of data points fall.
Linear regression and R2 coefficient.
To assess how well the estimated statistical pa-
rameters match the reference, a simple linear regression of the form y = ax+b was fitted
using the numpy.polyfit function, which provides the slope a and intercept b. The coeffi-
cient of determination, R2, was then computed as the square of the correlation coefficient
obtained from numpy.corrcoef. The value of R2 expresses how much of the variation in
the estimated data can be explained by the reference data, with R2 = 1 representing a
perfect linear relationship (The Pennsylvania State University, n.d.).
Figure 5: Workflow for constructing encounter–frame response spectra and comparing
them in two domains. Case A uses semi-analytical TRFs; Case B uses RAOs from DNV
Wasim; ground truth spectra are estimated from accelerations using csd. Conversion
between domains uses Saa(f) = (2πf)4Sηη(f) and Sηη(f) = Saa(f)/(2πf)4. Outliers are
identified from the ground truth in each domain and the corresponding samples are filtered
out from all the cases – Cases A and B, as well as ground truth. A cut-off at 0.3 [Hz] is
used throughout.

==PAGE:31==
5
Analysis
This section presents the results of the spectral comparison between the model-based
approaches (Case A and Case B) and the measured ground truth (GT). Two analysis
domains are considered: the displacement domain, representing the vessel’s physical
motions, and the acceleration domain, which enables direct comparison with the mea-
sured onboard signals.
The evaluation covers three degrees of freedom — heave, pitch, and roll — and combines
correlation analysis with distribution-based error metrics. Scatter plots are used to com-
pare individual spectral parameters (Hm0/2, Tm01, Tp, and Tz), while the overall spectral
agreement is quantified using the Normalised Integrated Absolute Error (NIAE).
Prior to the analysis, outliers were removed based on the GT data to exclude unrealistic
responses. In total, 214 samples were filtered out from the displacement-domain analysis
and 349 from the acceleration-domain analysis, leaving 4689 and 4554 valid samples,
respectively.
The section is structured as follows: first, results for the displacement domain are pre-
sented, followed by those for the acceleration domain. Finally, additional studies, includ-
ing parameter optimisation and selected response spectrum examples, are discussed.
5.1
Response spectra on displacement basis
This subsection presents the results for the displacement domain, where motions are ex-
pressed as heave [m], pitch [rad], and roll [rad]. Scatter plots of the main spectral param-
eters illustrate the correlation between the models and GT, complemented by NIAE distri-
butions summarising the overall spectral similarity. The results are discussed separately
for each degree of freedom, and the corresponding figures are shown in Figures 6–9.
5.1.1
Displacement-based correlations
This part focuses on the correlation between the model-based and measured response
spectra in the displacement domain. The analysis covers the main spectral parameters —
amplitude (Hm0/2), mean period (Tm01), peak period (Tp), and zero-crossing period (Tz)
— for heave, pitch, and roll. The corresponding scatter plots are shown in Figures 6–9,
and the results are discussed for each degree of freedom separately.
In Figure 6 can be seen that for heave and pitch, both Case A and Case B show gen-
erally good agreement with the ground truth, although Case B performs better in both.
The situation is different for roll. Case A systematically underpredicts the amplitudes, re-
sulting in values that remain almost constant and only match a few of the very smallest
GT amplitudes. It appears insensitive to the input — the waves, the heading or speed.
In contrast, Case B follows the overall trend more closely, but with increasing scatter at
higher amplitudes. This spreading suggests that when Case B predicts high amplitudes,
the GT values are often smaller, and when GT amplitudes are high, Case B tends to
underestimate them. Nevertheless, a portion of the points still lie along the identity line
(x = y), showing that correct matches occur occasionally.

and roll in displacement-based analysis (Cases A and B).

and roll in displacement-based analysis (Cases A and B).
Figure 7 depicts that in heave and pitch, both Case A and Case B show reasonably good
agreement with the ground truth, with Case B again performing slightly better overall. A
number of points fall outside the main cluster, which indicates occasional mismatches
between the estimates and GT. These are mostly cases of overprediction, although a few
underpredicted values also appear. For roll, the results remain poor in both cases. Here,
Tm01 is systematically underpredicted, and the spread increases with higher GT values,
leading to larger variations at longer periods.

For heave and pitch, as seen in Figure 8, both cases capture the peak period to a reason-
able degree, with Case B again performing slightly better overall. A clear banding pattern
is visible in the scatter plots, and there is strong spreading around the identity line. This
indicates that the models sometimes overpredict and sometimes underpredict the values.
Since the deviations occur in both directions, the trendlines stay fairly close to the identity
line, even though the overall agreement isn’t very strong. For roll, the results are poor in
both cases. Here, Tp is consistently underpredicted, and the scatter is again structured in

bands rather than showing a clear correlation with GT.

Finally, Figure 9 shows that for heave and pitch, both cases capture the zero-crossing
period reasonably well, with Case B aligning more closely with GT. Some scatter appears
at higher GT values, mostly due to overprediction, although a few underpredicted cases
are also visible. For roll, the agreement remains poor, with both cases underpredicting Tz
and showing pronounced scatter at larger values.

Heave.
For amplitudes (Hm0/2), Case A underestimates the scale (slope ≈0.74), while
Case B is closer to one-to-one (slope ≈0.90). Both correlate well with GT (R2 = 0.83
and 0.86), but Case B has lower errors (MAE = 0.12 [m], RMSE = 0.17 [m]) than Case A
(MAE = 0.22 [m], RMSE = 0.28 [m]). For the mean period (Tm01), both reach R2 ≈0.70,
with smaller errors for Case B (MAE = 0.91 [s], RMSE = 1.38 [s]) than Case A (MAE =
1.37 [s], RMSE = 1.72 [s]). The zero-crossing period (Tz) shows the same pattern, with
Case B closer to GT (MAE = 0.79 [s] vs 1.32 [s]). For the peak period (Tp), correlations
are weak (R2 ≈0.35) and errors are large in both cases (MAE = 3.23–3.38 [s], RMSE =
4.51–5.03 [s]).
Pitch.
For amplitudes (Hm0/2), Case A underestimates the scale (slope ≈0.64, R2 =
0.87), whereas Case B is close to one-to-one (slope ≈0.99, R2 = 0.89) and shows lower
errors (MAE = 0.002 [rad] vs 0.004 [rad], RMSE = 0.003 [rad] vs 0.005 [rad]). For Tm01
and Tz, both give R2 ≈0.80, with Case B again lower in error (MAE = 0.86–0.79 [s],
RMSE = 1.30–1.23 [s]) than Case A (MAE = 1.36–1.34 [s], RMSE = 1.84–1.86 [s]). For
Tp, agreement is moderate (R2 ≈0.56–0.57) and errors remain large (MAE = 3.06–3.39
[s], RMSE = 4.42–4.72 [s]), with a slight advantage for Case B.
Roll.
For amplitudes (Hm0/2), Case A produces almost constant values (slope ≈0, R2
= 0.17), while Case B shows limited scaling (slope ≈0.31, R2 = 0.13). For the periods
(Tm01, Tz, Tp), both cases perform poorly, with R2 < 0.20 and large errors (MAE = 6.5–7.6
[s], RMSE up to 8.8 [s]). This confirms that roll is not captured reliably by either approach.

5.1.2
Displacement-based NIAE distributions
In addition to the scatter plot correlations, the overall agreement between the estimated
and GT spectra was quantified using the Normalised Integrated Absolute Error (NIAE).
Figures 10–12 show the NIAE distributions for heave, pitch, and roll in displacement-
based analysis, comparing Case A (semi-analytical TRFs) and Case B (RAOs). The
vertical solid lines mark the mean values, while the dashed lines indicate the 90th per-
centiles.
It should be noted that the histograms are plotted with an upper axis limit of 10. The bin
widths differ between figures, as they are based on the maximum calculated values for
each case: 100 bins are distributed across the range up to the maximum. Thus, narrower
bins indicate that the spread of errors was smaller (lower maxima), while wider bins reflect
larger spreads with higher maximum values. For completeness, histograms using the full
ranges are included in Appendix 5.
Figure 10: Distribution of NIAE values for heave (displacement-based analysis). Solid
lines show mean values; dashed lines indicate 90th percentiles.
As can be seen in Figure 10 for heave motion, Case A matches the GT better, with the
mean NIAE of 0.68 versus 0.72 in Case B. The 90th percentile is slightly lower again for
Case A (0.84) compared to Case B (0.99), indicating narrower spread of errors in Case A.
For pitch, as presented in Figure 11, Case A led to a better match against GT. The mean
NIAE value is 0.72, while in Case B it is 0.73. The Q90 is equal to 0.87 in Case A, and
1.03 for the second case. However, the distribution tail is heavier for Case B, with the
90th percentile at 1.03, compared to 0.87 for Case A.
For roll, the discrepancies are large, see Figure 12. In Case A, the results reduce to a
mean NIAE of about 1.0, with the same value also at the 90th percentile. This happens
because Case A barely reacts in roll — the spectra are almost flat, so the total error
that NIAE captures stays small, even though the match with GT is clearly poor. Case B
behaves differently. The distribution is much broader, with a mean NIAE of 2.36 and a
90th percentile of 4.61. This shows that Case B does capture more variation in roll, but
at the cost of much larger deviations from GT.

by the definition of NIAE. That is, the roll responses are very small and constant (flat), so
the total error stays small once divided/normalised by the GT spectral energy. Case B, on
the other hand, captures more variation in roll, which leads to a broader distribution and
higher NIAE values.

The comparison in Figure 13 illustrates how the transformation from displacement to ac-
celeration affects the spectral characteristics. For sample 2800, the roll response is almost
negligible in the acceleration domain but becomes large after transforming to the motion
domain, indicating that low-frequency components gain relative importance during this
conversion. In contrast, the heave and pitch spectra flatten slightly in the motion domain.
For sample 200, the roll amplitude is nearly absent in the acceleration domain; however,
after converting to motion, a small peak appears, shifted toward lower frequencies. This
shift results from dividing by (2πf)4 at low frequencies, which amplifies minor variations
in that range. These examples highlight how the (2πf)4 weighting amplifies differences at
higher frequencies and can visually alter the apparent response characteristics. A slightly
stronger smoothing could help reduce the spikiness observed in the pitch response.
5.2
Response spectra on acceleration basis
This section presents the results in the acceleration domain, where responses are ex-
pressed as heave [m/s2], pitch [rad/s2], and roll [rad/s2]. Unlike the displacement anal-
ysis, this domain enables a direct comparison with the measured onboard signals. The
same evaluation procedure is applied: first, correlations of the main spectral parameters
(Hm0/2, Tm01, Tp, and Tz) are analysed, followed by NIAE distributions that assess the
overall spectral agreement.
5.2.1
Acceleration-based correlations
The estimated response parameters from Case A and Case B are compared with the
ground truth (GT) for heave, pitch, and roll. The corresponding scatter plots are shown in
Figures 14–17, and the results are discussed separately for each degree of freedom.

patterns, which repeat across the three DOFs. This indicates that the overestimation is
systematic rather than random, and affects both methods in a comparable way.

5.3
Parameter optimisation
5.3.1
NIAE optimisation analysis
The final step of the study was to test whether the agreement with GT could be improved
by adjusting the input parameters (L, B, T, µ, and GMT ) through an optimisation proce-
dure. The idea was to loop over a broad set of parameter values, compare the resulting
spectra to the GT, and then select those parameters that give the lowest error. Since
this is a model-based calculation, there is no restriction that the optimised parameters
have to remain physically realistic. In principle, any “reasonable” or “extreme” parameter
combination could be acceptable, as long as it improves the match to GT. The aim of the
optimisation was purely to get closer to the reference, not necessarily to preserve physical
meaning.
The optimisation was based on the Normalised Integrated Absolute Error (NIAE). For
each parameter combination, NIAE was calculated for heave, pitch, and roll, and the
three values were then averaged to obtain a single overall score. This means that the
optimisation aimed to find one common parameter set that gives the best combined match
across all three motions rather than treating them separately. The assumption was that
by matching the overall spectral shapes more closely (i.e., achieving a lower mean NIAE),
the statistical parameters would also align better. However, minimising NIAE alone can
distort the spectra in ways that worsen the physical accuracy. The metric penalises total
spectral differences but does not ensure that key response features, such as the peak
position or energy distribution, are matched correctly, as seen for roll in Figure 12.
The optimisation was carried out by looping over different sets of input parameters. A
wide range of values was included at first, some close to those used in the main analysis
and others deliberately more extreme, such as L = 420 [m], T = 60 [m], or GMT = 20 [m].
The first runs were performed on random subsets of the data, initially with 50 samples,
later increased to 100 and 200. Because the initial parameter ranges were broad, these
runs were time-consuming. To make later tests faster, parameter values that consistently
performed poorly were removed, leaving a narrower range to test in the following runs.
This made the computation faster but also meant that potentially useful combinations
might have been excluded.
The best-performing parameters from the optimisation runs were then applied to the full
dataset (over 4000 samples) to evaluate their general performance. Running the full opti-
misation directly on all data was avoided due to the long computational time. The results
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
42
==PAGE:49==
turned out to be poor — optimising against NIAE did not lead to lower errors, neither in
NIAE itself nor in the statistical parameters. The resulting spectra often appeared flat-
tened, with underestimated peaks and shapes that differed from GT. This shows that the
NIAE-based optimisation did not produce useful or physically meaningful improvements.
For completeness, results from this optimisation are shown in Figures 22–28. The pa-
rameter set used for the full run was L = 350 [m], B = 130 [m], T = 30 [m], µ = 0.9 [–], and
GMT = 10 [m].
The results presented in Figures 22–28 compare the optimised Case A directly with Case B,
while Tables 9–11 summarise the performance relative to the original Case A.
Figure 22: Correlation between estimated and GT amplitudes (Hm0/2) for heave, pitch,
and roll in acceleration-based analysis (Optimised A and Case B).

The NIAE distributions in Table 11 also do not suggest any improvement, except for roll.

the original Case A to 1.00 after optimisation, and the 90th percentile from 1.9 to 1.00.

method tends to alter the spectra globally without preserving the key statistical features
that describe the motion response.
5.3.2

the statistical behaviour of the spectra in displacement domain rather than to minimise
NIAE. This was not an automated search, a few nearby combinations around Case A
were plotted and compared visually, with particular attention to roll. The final set used
was L = 230 [m], B = 30 [m], T = 13 [m], µ = 0.2 [–], and GMT = 7 [m].

Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
