==PAGE:64==
6
Discussion
This section discusses the main findings of the analysis, with focus on how the different
modelling approaches performed, how the results differ between the displacement and
acceleration domains, and what the main sources of discrepancies are. The discussion
also outlines the main limitations and points to possible directions for future work.
6.1
Overview of results
The comparison between the semi-analytical Case A, the numerical Case B, and the mea-
sured reference (GT) shows that both modelling approaches capture the overall behaviour
in heave and pitch reasonably well, although some deviations remain. Roll, however, is
poorly predicted in all cases. Case B, based on the Rankine panel method RAOs, per-
forms slightly better overall, particularly in amplitude estimation. Nevertheless, consider-
ing its simplicity and limited input requirements, Case A still achieves a surprisingly good
agreement for heave and pitch.
Case A relies on simplified theoretical relations that assume linear response and neglect
nonlinear effects. Viscous effects are only included in a simplified way through an empiri-
cal correction to roll damping, which does not fully capture the actual physical behaviour.
These assumptions are acceptable for heave and pitch in moderate seas but not for roll
motion, which is strongly affected by nonlinear and viscous mechanisms. Case B includes
hydrodynamic interactions through the panel method. However, it neglects viscous damp-
ing unless it is explicitly added. As a result, the roll responses in both approaches remain
far from the GT results, with Case A underestimating the amplitude and Case B overpre-
dicting.
6.2
Displacement and acceleration domains
Two domains were analysed to evaluate the performance of both methods from different
perspectives. The displacement domain represents the physical motions of the vessel
and shows how the modelled responses compare in terms of actual motion amplitudes.
The acceleration domain, on the other hand, allows direct comparison with the measured
signals, since the reference data were obtained from acceleration sensors.
The conversion between the two domains was handled through Saa = (2πf)4Sηη, which
amplifies discrepancies at higher frequencies. This effect is visible in the results, where
differences that appear minor in displacement become more distinct in acceleration. The
transformation therefore helps to highlight inconsistencies in the spectral shape and en-
ergy distribution, even though the general trends between the domains remain consistent.
Heave and pitch results follow similar tendencies across both domains, while roll shows
a large increase in variability after the transformation.
When converting in the opposite direction — from measured acceleration spectra (GT) to
displacement — the inverse relation Sηη = Saa/(2πf)4 was applied. At low frequencies,
the denominator becomes very small, so even minor variations in the acceleration data
can create artificial energy or distort the low-frequency range. This transformation also
affects the overall spectral shape: multiplying by (2πf)4 in the forward direction steepens
the spectra and shifts the peak slightly toward higher frequencies, while dividing by it in the
reverse direction flattens the response and moves the peak toward longer periods. Be-
cause dividing by small frequency values amplifies irregularities, unrealistic peaks may
appear in the long-period range. This behaviour was most visible in roll, where the re-
sponses are narrow-banded and sensitive to damping. In such cases, most of the energy
is concentrated around the natural roll frequency, which makes the response highly sensi-
tive to small changes in damping or encounter frequency. It also explains why the spread
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
58
==PAGE:65==
of Tp values was larger in the motion domain. Still, the overall behaviour between the two
domains remained consistent, suggesting that these differences were mainly caused by
the transformation rather than random variation.
6.3
Interpretation of spectral parameters
The statistical parameters highlight different aspects of how well the response spectra are
represented. The amplitude parameter (Hm0/2) shows how accurately the total response
energy is captured. Case B generally aligns better with GT, giving a more consistent esti-
mate of the overall energy level in both domains. Case A performs reasonably for heave
and pitch, although the amplitudes are slightly lower than in GT. In roll, the amplitudes in
the motion domain are very small, suggesting that the response energy is not captured
correctly. This can be partly attributed to the simplified formulation introduced by J. J.
Jensen et al. (2004), but also to the uncertainty in the input parameters, especially the
assumed values of µ and GMT . After transforming to the acceleration domain, the roll re-
sponse appears stronger, since the higher-frequency weighting brings out variations that
were barely visible in displacement. Case B tends to overpredict roll amplitudes, mainly
because viscous damping is not included in the panel method, which leads to exagger-
ated peaks, especially after the acceleration-domain transformation. These patterns show
how the transformation between domains leads to different effects on discrepancies, even
though their underlying causes remain the same.
The period parameters (Tm01, Tz, and Tp) capture different characteristics of the response
spectra. While Tm01 and Tz based on the overall energy distribution, Tp represents the fre-
quency at which the main energy peak occurs within that distribution. These parameters
depend more strongly on the detailed spectral shape and are therefore more sensitive to
modelling differences.
In the motion domain, both cases show a reasonable match with GT for heave and pitch,
although the scatter remains noticeable. The roll periods are generally shorter than in
GT, especially for Case A. This is consistent with the underestimated response energy
in roll, where the missing low-frequency energy causes the main spectral peak to shift
slightly toward higher frequencies. The strong banding and spread observed in Tp suggest
that the peak location was not always identified consistently. Even though the frequency
resolution was increased and a polynomial fit was used to refine the peak estimate, these
steps did not remove the issue completely. This indicates that the mismatch likely comes
from how the spectral shape itself was reproduced rather than from the peak-detection
method.
In the acceleration domain, the banding effect is no longer visible, and the overall distribu-
tions appear more compact. However, there is still noticeable scatter toward higher period
values in both Case A and B. This behaviour results from the (2πf)4 weighting, which in-
creases the influence of higher frequencies and shifts the energy distribution toward that
range. The transformation makes the peaks appear sharper and the results seem more
consistent, but this is mostly a visual effect rather than an actual improvement, since the
same differences remain underneath.
When analysing the period parameters in the acceleration domain, their interpretation
becomes less reliable. Even though the same formulas are used, multiplying by (2πf)4
changes how the energy across frequencies is weighted. This affects the spectral mo-
ments, especially for Tm01 and Tz, and the resulting periods no longer represent the actual
timing of the motions. The peak period Tp is not based on moments, and even though the
results may look better, this is due to the transformation and not because the underlying
spectra match more closely.
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
59
==PAGE:66==
When converting in the opposite direction, from acceleration to motion, some uncertainty
is introduced into the GT data. This comes from dividing by small frequency values, which
can amplify small irregularities in the low-frequency range. Nevertheless, comparing the
cases in the motion domain gives a more realistic picture of the responses and a clearer
physical interpretation. While the acceleration domain ensures consistency with the mea-
surements, it does not seem to be a suitable choice for analysing the statistical parameters
of the spectra.
6.4
NIAE comparison across domains
The NIAE results show that both cases perform on a comparable level, although their
relative ranking changes between the domains. In the motion domain, Case A gives
slightly lower mean and 90th percentile values for heave and pitch than Case B, indicat-
ing a closer overall agreement with GT. After transforming to the acceleration domain,
the situation reverses, and Case B shows slightly lower errors. This change is caused by
the (2πf)4 weighting, which increases the influence of high frequencies. Since Case A
underestimates energy in that region, its errors become more pronounced after the trans-
formation. Case B, on the other hand, slightly overestimates the higher-frequency range,
so the relative difference to GT does not increase as much. As a result, its performance
appears slightly improved in the acceleration domain, even though the underlying mis-
match remains the same.
For roll, the differences are much larger. In both domains, Case A shows low NIAE values,
but this does not mean that the spectra match well. The responses are very small in both
GT and Case A, which drives the normalised error toward unity regardless of the actual
agreement. Case B produces much larger roll amplitudes, which are then further amplified
by the transformation, resulting in much higher NIAE values in acceleration.
The comparison also shows that NIAE behaves more consistently across the two domains
than the statistical parameters. The overall magnitudes of the errors remain within a
similar range, which suggests that NIAE can be applied in both motion and acceleration
domains, provided that the influence of the frequency weighting is kept in mind. It remains
a practical way to compare overall spectral agreement, but it should always be viewed
alongside other measures to confirm that the agreement is also physically meaningful.
6.5
Roll response
The roll motion is the most challenging to predict, and both approaches fail to represent
it accurately. The main reason lies in the physical nature of roll, which is heavily influ-
enced by viscous damping, bilge keels, and nonlinear restoring effects. None of these
are explicitly captured in the linear frameworks used in this thesis.
In Case A, the simplified formulation links the roll response to the main vessel dimensions
and a few tuning parameters. The viscous damping is partly accounted for through the
empirical correction in the roll damping term B44, scaled by the factor µ, but this treat-
ment is highly simplified and does not reproduce the actual physical behaviour, especially
near resonance. Case B, based on the Rankine panel method, solves the radiation–
diffraction problem on a panelised wetted surface with linearised boundary conditions.
In such formulations, viscous effects are not inherently represented and must be added
through empirical or supplementary damping models. It is uncertain whether such addi-
tional damping was included in the RAOs used in this work, but the high roll amplitudes
suggest that it was either omitted or not sufficiently captured.
Including nonlinear damping or empirically based viscous roll terms would likely improve
the overall representation. This remains one of the main limitations of the current linear
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
60
==PAGE:67==
framework and a key direction for future work.
6.6
Optimisation analysis
The optimisation attempts highlighted several important drawbacks and limitations. While
the procedure aimed to minimise the overall NIAE, the resulting spectra often became
distorted, showing flattened peaks and reduced amplitudes. This suggests that improving
the numerical value of NIAE does not necessarily bring the spectra closer in a physical
sense, as the metric can be lowered simply by reducing the total energy content rather
than matching the spectral shape.
Another drawback was the high computational cost, which limited the optimisation to
smaller subsets of data. As a result, the identified parameter combinations were not rep-
resentative of the full dataset and did not generalise well when applied to all samples. This
also shows that to obtain meaningful results, the optimisation should ideally be performed
on the full studied dataset.
The tests showed that when one response component improved, another often became
worse. For instance, when the roll behaviour improved, the heave and pitch errors typ-
ically increased. This is because the parameters in the semi-analytical formulation are
closely connected — changing L, B, or T to better match one motion also affects the
others, but not necessarily in a favourable way. The optimisation therefore becomes a
matter of trade-offs rather than straightforward improvement.
It also became clear that the type of target plays an important role. Minimising NIAE
can give better numerical agreement, but it does not guarantee that the spectral shape
itself becomes more realistic. Adjusting the statistical parameters instead helps target
the physical characteristics of the response, but even small changes in one direction can
shift others away from their best fit. Meaningful results would likely require optimisation
on the full dataset and a clearly defined objective — whether improving general spectral
similarity or focusing on specific motion components.
6.7
Limitations
Several limitations influence the results and should be kept in mind when interpreting
them. The draught and GMT of the vessel were not known and had to be estimated,
which adds uncertainty to the transfer functions, especially in roll. The RAO data used
in Case B were available only for a single draught and a limited range of speeds, and
the calculations do not include nonlinear effects. The ship speed in the analysis was
rounded to the nearest available value, which has a small but noticeable influence on the
comparison.
The ERA5 wave spectra inherently contain modelling uncertainty, as discussed in the
dataset description. In addition, the spectra were taken from the grid point closest to
the vessel’s position, which in some cases was up to about 30 km away. This creates a
spatial offset, but direct spatial interpolation was not applied. Interpolating wave spectra
in space is not straightforward, since it can easily distort the energy balance, and a more
advanced approach would be needed to do this correctly. Time interpolation between
ERA5 outputs was, however, performed. This approach can introduce small errors but is
commonly used and considered acceptable in practice.
Other small sources of uncertainty include the interpolation of RAO data across frequency,
frequency regridding, and directional wrapping during preprocessing. These effects can
accumulate and contribute to the total scatter seen in the results. Some uncertainty is
also introduced through the spectral estimation procedure itself, since parameters such as
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
61
==PAGE:68==
windowing, smoothing, or the number of segments influence the resulting cross-spectral
densities. Finally, part of the error is expected to come from the measurements them-
selves, for example due to sensor noise.
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
62
==PAGE:69==
7
Conclusion
This study investigated the accuracy of model-based predictions of wave-induced ship
motions, with emphasis on the roll response due to its often nonlinear and poorly captured
nature. Two modelling approaches were compared: Case A, based on semi-analytical
transfer functions, and Case B, employing Response Amplitude Operators (RAOs) ob-
tained from a commercial potential flow solver. Both were combined with directional wave
spectra derived from ERA5 reanalysis data to estimate response spectra for heave, pitch,
and roll, and subsequently validated against measured acceleration-based ground truth
data.
Both approaches reproduced the measured motion responses with reasonable accuracy,
particularly for heave and pitch. The agreement was generally better for Case B, which
consistently yielded higher correlations and lower error measures, showing that the more
advanced hydrodynamic model provides a more realistic representation of ship response.
However, the semi-analytical Case A also performed well, suggesting that simplified trans-
fer functions can still capture the main motion characteristics when combined with realis-
tic environmental input such as ERA5 sea states. The use of wave spectra derived from
ERA5 reanalysis likely contributed to this close match, suggesting that accurate sea state
representation can partly compensate for model simplifications.
The roll response remained the most difficult to reproduce, with both approaches showing
low correlations and significant deviations in amplitude and period parameters. This lim-
itation can be attributed to the linear assumptions of both models, which neglect viscous
damping and other nonlinear effects. The comparison also illustrated how the level of
hydrodynamic detail and the representation of damping affect the accuracy of response
spectra, particularly for roll.
The analysis further examined the sensitivity of the results to variations in input parame-
ters and explored two optimisation strategies. The optimisation based on the Normalised
Integrated Absolute Error (NIAE) did not improve the agreement and instead flattened the
spectral shapes, indicating that minimising spectral distance alone can distort key mo-
tion characteristics. A secondary optimisation based on statistical parameters, instead of
NIAE, led to a partial improvement in the roll amplitude, bringing it closer to the measured
reference. However, this came at the cost of slightly larger errors in heave and pitch,
confirming that enhancing one response component may compromise others.
7.1
Broader implications
The findings show that both model-based approaches have distinct advantages and are
suited for different applications. Case A, being computationally efficient and requiring lim-
ited input while still performing well, is appropriate for preliminary design stages or large-
scale statistical analyses, as also documented by J. J. Jensen et al. (2004). Case B,
although more demanding, provides greater detail and accuracy, making it suitable for
focused performance evaluations. The generally good agreement between ERA5-driven
predictions and measured responses highlights the potential of reanalysis data as a prac-
tical foundation for response estimation and probabilistic assessment of ship motions in
realistic sea conditions.
7.2
Future work
Several aspects could be explored to improve and extend the present framework. A nat-
ural next step would be to include nonlinear and viscous effects, particularly in roll, where
the linear assumptions are insufficient to capture the physical damping behaviour. Such
extensions would also allow for a more realistic representation of the ship’s dynamics
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
63
==PAGE:70==
in severe sea states, where nonlinearities and transient responses become more pro-
nounced.
Further improvement could be achieved by using time-dependent hydrostatic input, such
as the actual draught and GMT corresponding to each measured condition, rather than
relying on assumed constant values. Including the vessel’s true speed for each case
would also enhance the accuracy of the encounter frequency and resulting response pre-
dictions.
The optimisation procedure could be developed into a more detailed study, using multi-
objective or degree-of-freedom–specific approaches to identify parameter combinations
that improve multiple response components simultaneously. Finally, the influence of fre-
quency and directional resolution on the resulting spectra could be investigated in greater
detail, to better understand how interpolation and discretisation choices affect both model-
and measurement-based results.

2
Supplementary derivation: Roll excitation moment
The roll excitation moment can be derived from the Haskind relation, which connects
the diffraction problem to the corresponding radiation problem. In this framework, the
sectional excitation moment m(x) is expressed in terms of the sectional hydrodynamic
damping b44 (Newman, 1963, 2017).
In his original treatment of the two-dimensional case, Newman (1963) considered plane
waves incident on an infinitely long cylinder. By enclosing the body with a large control
contour — consisting of the free surface, the seabed at z = −∞, and two vertical planes
at x →±∞— the surface-integral form of the Haskind relation reduces to a line integral
at infinity. This highlights that the exciting force in each mode depends solely on the far-
field radiation potentials. Moreover, Newman (1963) noted that while in general the two
radiation functions P +
4 and P −
4 are unequal, for bodies symmetric about the transverse
axis their magnitudes become equal. For roll this symmetry gives P +
4 = −P −
4 , analogous
to surge and pitch.

relation and, within the framework of linear wave theory, it is exact for any symmetric
cross-section (Newman, 1963).
Carrying out the integration gives the closed form (J. J. Jensen et al., 2004)

A(x, β) = sin β e−ixk cos β eiωt
can be interpreted as the spatial–temporal modulation of the excitation amplitude, while
the square-root factor encodes the coupling with the damping coefficient. This matches
the interpretation given in Newman (1963): the exciting force is proportional to the ampli-
tude of the radiated wave at infinity, in the direction of wave incidence.
In beam seas (β = 90◦), this reduces to the classical result reported by Newman (2017)(J. J.
Jensen et al., 2004). More generally, Equation (A.5) illustrates the exact relation that links
excitation and damping for two-dimensional symmetric bodies.
The total excitation moment M for the entire ship is obtained by integrating the sectional
contributions along the length. In the two-beam approximation (J. J. Jensen et al., 2004),
the sectional damping is taken as piecewise constant: b44,0 on section 0 (0 ≤x ≤δL) and
b44,1 on section 1 (δL ≤x ≤L), with δ denoting the longitudinal partitioning parameter.

Speed effect on TRFs
This supplementary study was performed to evaluate the influence of speed rounding
on transfer functions. The calculations follow the semi-analytical formulas presented by
J. J. Jensen et al. (2004). The setup does not involve response spectra, but instead
quantifies the difference between transfer functions computed for actual recorded speeds
(with decimals between 16.4 and 21.4 kn) and for the corresponding rounded integer
speeds. Since transfer functions depend not only on speed but also on heading, the
directional aspect was included by considering headings from 0° to 350° in steps of 10°.
The applied hydrodynamic model parameters were µ = 0.4, δ = 0.62, and GMt = 1.15,
while the remaining values were taken from the main ship particulars given earlier.
For each heading, the discrepancy between the two cases was measured using the root-
mean-square error (RMSE) across the frequency axis. The resulting RMSE distributions
are illustrated in Figures A.2a–A.2c, while Tables A.2 and A.1 summarize the numerical
results. For heave and pitch, the error is largest in head and following seas (β = 0° and
180°) and vanishes in beam seas (β = 90° and 270°) as a direct consequence of the cos β
dependence in the encounter frequency. For roll, the RMSE is zero in beam seas and
reaches a maximum around β = 60° and 300°. The values in Table A.1 are calculated from
the average RMSE and the average TRF amplitude across all headings and frequencies.
This shows the average level of difference.

Figure A.25: Correlation between estimated and GT zero-crossing periods (Tz) for heave,
pitch, and roll in acceleration-based analysis (Optimised A and Case B).
For the amplitudes (Hm0/2), the slopes for heave and pitch changed only slightly, while
roll showed a clear improvement (slope 0.38 →0.75). The R2 values stayed at about the
same level, meaning that the overall match with the ground truth remained similar, but the
roll amplitude scaling improved noticeably.
For the period metrics (Tm01, Tz, Tp), no systematic improvement was observed. Most
slopes and R2 values decreased slightly—for example, the heave Tm01 slope fell from
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
103
==PAGE:110==
0.13 to 0.08, and the pitch Tz slope changed from -0.27 to -0.36. Adjusting parameters to
produce “cleaner-looking” spectra did not translate into better period estimates.

7
Project plan
English title: Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
Student: Veronika Vajaýová (212688) Start date: 2025-05-05 ECTS Points: 30 Supervi-
sor/s: Ulrik Dam Nielsen (udni@dtu.dk) Raphaël Emile Gilbert Mounet (regmo@dtu.dk)
Content: In this project, the student will analyze data and measurements from a container
vessel in cross-Atlantic service (Nielsen et al., 2024). The project will utilize response
measurements collected during multiple voyages, along with weather data from the Euro-
pean Centre for Medium-Range Weather Forecasts (ECMWF). The objective is to assess
the accuracy of model-based estimates of wave-induced responses, with special attention
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
107
==PAGE:114==
to roll motion due to its potential for nonlinear behavior. The project includes the following
tasks: 1. Implement Response Amplitude Operators (RAOs) for a simplified ship geom-
etry, available as closed-form expressions (Jensen et al., 2004). 2. Develop and use a
computational model to estimate the ship’s response using the RAOs from point 1, com-
bined with directional wave spectra from reanalysis weather data. 3. Repeat point 2,
but using another available set of RAOs obtained via a commercial potential flow solver.
Compare the estimates with those from point 2. 4. Process and perform spectral analysis
of the measured responses during the containership voyages. 5. Validate the computa-
tional model by comparing its outputs with the measured response spectra from point 4.
6. Perform a sensitivity analysis to determine the impact of various model parameters on
the response estimates. 7. Investigate the influence of severe sea states on the ship’s
dynamic behavior (optional).
  Background and motivation Understanding the dynamic behavior of ships in realistic
sea conditions is critical for good seakeeping and structural design. While predictions
of heave and pitch motions tend to be more reliable, the roll motion presents challenges
due to its sensitivity to nonlinear effects and potential for resonance in beam seas. In
recent years, the growing availability of onboard measurement data, alongside weather
observations, has improved opportunities to validate and refine the analytical models and
computational tools used to estimate wave-induced ship responses. This project uses
motion measurements from a transatlantic container vessel operated by DNV. The initial
phase includes a warm-up exercise aimed at developing a foundation in applying theo-
retical ship motion models. This includes implementing closed-form transfer functions for
heave, pitch, and roll, following the approach by Jensen et al. (2004), which assumes
a simplified ship geometry. Directional wave spectra (2D) are obtained from the ERA5
dataset and processed into point spectra (1D). From these, statistical data and ship re-
sponse spectra are calculated. The influence of chosen model parameter on roll behavior
is also examined.
Objectives This study aims to investigate the accuracy of model-based predictions of
wave-induced motions, placing particular emphasis on roll due to its complex, often non-
linear nature. By combining theoretical modeling with real sea state data, the goal is to
assess how well existing methods capture the dynamic behavior of ships under realis-
tic operating conditions, and to identify factors that influence the accuracy of response
estimates.
Methodology First, Response Amplitude Operators (RAOs) for heave, pitch, and roll mo-
tions are implemented based on closed-form expressions for a simplified ship geometry,
as described in Jensen et al. (2004). These expressions serve as a baseline represen-
tation of the vessel’s motion characteristics under wave excitation. Next, a computational
model is developed to estimate the vessel’s dynamic response. This is done by integrating
the implemented RAOs with directional wave spectra derived from ERA5 reanalysis data.
The same computational framework is used with an alternative set of RAOs generated
by a commercial potential flow solver. The resulting ship responses are compared with
those obtained using the analytical RAOs, allowing for an evaluation of the differences
between simplified and numerically-derived models. In parallel, motion data measured
onboard the DNV container vessel during its voyages are processed. Spectral analysis
is performed to extract the observed response spectra, providing a reference for validat-
ing the model-based estimates. Validation of the computational model is carried out by
comparing the simulated response spectra with those derived from measured data. This
step enables the assessment of how well the models capture the real-life behavior of the
vessel. A sensitivity analysis is then performed to examine how variations in key model
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
108
==PAGE:115==
parameters affect the estimated responses, particularly for roll. Finally, the influence of
severe sea states on the ship’s motion behavior is explored as an optional extension of the
study. This provides insight into how extreme conditions may amplify dynamic responses.

# ---- Preparing spectral variables - full circle , frequency and omega

12
def prepare_spectrum_data(era5):
13
directions = era5.direction.values.astype(float)
14
if directions[-1] > 360.0 - 1e-9:
15
directions_360 = directions
16
d2fd = era5['d2fd']
17
else:
18

19

='direction')
20

21
22
directions_rad = np.deg2rad(directions_360)
23
f
= era5.frequency.values
24
omega = 2*np.pi*f
25
return d2fd, f, omega, directions_rad , directions_360

def geo_distance(lat1, lon1, lat2, lon2):
30
return geodesic((lat1, lon1), (lat2, lon2)).km

# ---- Closest ERA5 grid point via 4-candidate geodesic
----------------------------------------------------------------------
34
def get_closest_spectrum(ds, lat, lon, lat_grid , lon_grid):
35
# 2 nearest indices along each axis
36
lat_idx = np.abs(lat_grid - lat).argsort()[:2]
37
lon_idx = np.abs(lon_grid - lon).argsort()[:2]
38
39
candidate_points = []
40
distances = []
41
for i in lat_idx:
42
for j in lon_idx:
43
grid_lat = float(lat_grid[i])
44
grid_lon = float(lon_grid[j])
45
dist = geo_distance(lat, lon, grid_lat , grid_lon)
46
candidate_points.append((grid_lat , grid_lon))
47
distances.append(dist)
48
49
min_idx = int(np.argmin(distances))
50
closest_lat , closest_lon = candidate_points[min_idx]
51
52
53
spectrum = ds.sel(latitude=closest_lat , longitude=closest_lon)
54
55
56
spectrum = spectrum.assign_coords({'latitude': lat, 'longitude': lon})
57
58
59
spectrum.attrs['era5_grid_lat'] = closest_lat
60
spectrum.attrs['era5_grid_lon'] = closest_lon
61
spectrum.attrs['space_km']
= float(distances[min_idx])
62
63
return spectrum

# --- csd function --------------------------
29
def csd(x: np.ndarray):
30
f, Pxx = signal.csd(x, x, fs=fs, window=window , nperseg=nperseg ,
noverlap=noverlap , detrend='constant', return_onesided=True, scaling
='density')
31
return f, np.real(Pxx)

def load_rao_dat(path):
47
rao = np.loadtxt(path, comments='%')
#
loads the RAO txt file
48
f = rao[:, 0]
#
first column is frequency
49
50
block_cut = [0]
51
for i in range(1, len(f)):
52
if f[i] < f[i-1]:
53
block_cut.append(i)
#
vector of indexes , where one block end and another starts
54
block_cut.append(len(f))
#
adding last block (because the condition does not work as there is
no more f data)
55
56
number_of_blocks = len(block_cut) - 1
#
number of blocks
57
delta_beta = 360/number_of_blocks
58
beta = (np.arange(number_of_blocks) * delta_beta)
#
angle for which the block was calculated , 0 to 350 degrees , delta
beta is 10 deg -->> 36 blocks
59
freq = rao[block_cut[0]:block_cut[1], 0]
#
frequency range per block (all the blocks have the same frequency
range)
60
61
def reshaping_rao_structure(column_idx):
62
slices = []
63
for k in range(number_of_blocks):
64
block = rao[block_cut[k]:block_cut[k+1], column_idx]
65
slices.append(block)
66
rao_beta_vs_freq = np.stack(slices , axis=0)
#
creating an array of shape (beta, freq)
67
return rao_beta_vs_freq
68
69
return xr.Dataset(

def preparing_RAO_inputs_for_netsse(freq0, mu_deg , ws0_per_rad , ds_rao , dof,
target_freq_Hz=None):
89
mu = np.asarray(mu_deg, float).ravel()

91

96

# --- Jensen Transfer Functions --------------------------------
123
def relative_angle(ship_heading_deg , wave_direction_deg):
# calculating relative angle
between waves dir and heading
124
return ((wave_direction_deg - ship_heading_deg)) % 360
125
126
def Jensen_transfer_functions(beta_deg , omega, U, ship_params):
127
g
= ship_params["g"];
L
= ship_params["L"];
B
= ship_params["B
"];
T
= ship_params["T"]
128
Cb
= ship_params["Cb"];
Cwp = ship_params["Cwp"]; delta = ship_params[
"delta"]; mu_damp = ship_params["mu_damp"]
129
GMt = ship_params["GMt"]; rho
= ship_params["rho"]
130
131
beta_rad = np.radians(beta_deg)
132
B0 = B / Cb
133
C_Tn = 0.373 + 0.023*(B0/T) - 0.043*(L/100)
134
Tn = 2*C_Tn*B0/np.sqrt(GMt)
135
136
k = (omega**2) / g
137
k = np.where(k < 1e-100, 1e-100, k)
138
139
omega_e = omega - k*U*np.cos(beta_rad)
140
Fn = U / np.sqrt(g*L)
141
alpha = 1 - Fn*np.sqrt(k*L)*np.cos(beta_rad)
142
143
A = 2*np.sin(0.5*k*B*alpha**2) * np.exp(-k*T*alpha**2)
144
ke = np.abs(k*np.cos(beta_rad)); ke = np.where(ke < 1e-100, 1e-100, ke)
145
kappa = np.exp(-k*T)
146
ff = np.sqrt((1 - k*T)**2 + (A**2 / (k*B*alpha**3))**2)
147
148
F = np.abs(kappa*ff*(2/(ke*L))*np.sin(ke*L/2))
149
G = np.abs(kappa*ff*(24/(L*(ke*L)**2))*(np.sin(ke*L/2)-((ke*L/2)*np.cos(
ke*L/2))))
150
eta = 1/np.sqrt((1 - (2*k*T*alpha**2))**2 + (A**2/(k*B*alpha**2))**2)
151
152
heave = np.abs(eta*F)
153
pitch = np.abs(eta*G)
154
155
displ = L*T*B*rho
156
C44 = g*displ*GMt
157
gamma = np.clip((Cwp - delta)/(1 - delta), 1/6, 6)
158
B1 = gamma*(B / Cb)
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
119
==PAGE:126==
159
A0 = (Cb*(B/Cb)*T)/(delta + gamma*(1 - delta))
160
A1 = gamma*A0
161
B0_T = B0/T
162
B1_T = max(B1/T, 1.0)
163
164
if 3 <= B0_T <= 6:
aBT = 0.256*B0_T - 0.286; bBT = -0.11*B0_T - 2.55;
dBT = 0.033*B0_T - 1.419
165
elif 1 <= B0_T < 3:
aBT = -3.94*B0_T + 13.69;
bBT = -2.12*B0_T - 1.89;
dBT = 1.16*B0_T - 7.97
166
else:
aBT = bBT = dBT = 0.0
167
168
if 3 <= B1_T <= 6:
aBT1 = 0.256*B1_T - 0.286; bBT1 = -0.11*B1_T -
2.55; dBT1 = 0.033*B1_T - 1.419
169
elif 1 <= B1_T < 3:
aBT1 = -3.94*B1_T + 13.69;
bBT1 = -2.12*B1_T -
1.89; dBT1 = 1.16*B1_T - 7.97
170
else:
aBT1 = bBT1 = dBT1 = 0.0
171
172
omega_e = np.where(omega_e < 1e-10, 1e-10, omega_e)
173
b440 = aBT
* np.exp(bBT
* omega_e**(-1.3)) * omega_e**dBT
* (rho*A0*(
B/Cb)**2) * (1/np.sqrt((B/Cb)/(2*9.81)))
174
b440 = np.where(b440 < 1e-100, 1e-100, b440)
175
b441 = aBT1 * np.exp(bBT1 * omega_e**(-1.3)) * omega_e**dBT1 * (rho*A1*
B1**2) * (1/np.sqrt(B1/(2*9.81)))
176
kappab = np.sqrt(b441/b440)
177
B44 = L*b440*(delta + (kappab**2)*(1 - delta))
178
B44_star = (C44*Tn)/np.pi
179
B44_total = B44 + mu_damp*B44_star
180
181
M_real = np.abs(np.sin(beta_rad)) * np.sqrt((rho*9.81**2)/omega_e) * (2/
ke) * np.sqrt(b440) * (
182
(np.sin(0.5*delta*L*ke)**2 + kappab**2*np.sin(0.5*(1 - delta)*L*ke)
**2 +
183
2*kappab*np.sin(0.5*delta*L*ke)*np.sin(0.5*(1 - delta)*L*ke)*np.cos
(0.5*L*ke)))**0.5
184
roll = M_real / np.sqrt(((-omega_e**2*(Tn/(2*np.pi))**2 + 1)**2 * C44
**2) + (omega_e**2 * B44_total**2))
185
186
return heave.real, pitch.real, roll.real
187

# --- Jensen Transfer Functions applied to 2D
-----------------------------------
189
def Jensen_transfer_functions_2D(wave_directions_deg , omega, U, ship_params ,
ship_heading_deg):
190
nmu, nfreq = len(wave_directions_deg), len(omega)
191
beta_TRF = np.empty(nmu, dtype=float)
192
heave_Jensen_2D = np.zeros((nfreq, nmu), float)
193
pitch_Jensen_2D = np.zeros((nfreq, nmu), float)
194
roll_Jensen_2D
= np.zeros((nfreq, nmu), float)
195
for j, mu_deg in enumerate(wave_directions_deg):
196
beta = relative_angle(ship_heading_deg , mu_deg)
197
beta = (beta) % 360.0
198
beta_TRF[j] = beta
199
h, p, r = Jensen_transfer_functions(beta, omega, U, ship_params)
200
heave_Jensen_2D[:, j] = h; pitch_Jensen_2D[:, j] = p; roll_Jensen_2D
[:, j] = r
201
return heave_Jensen_2D , pitch_Jensen_2D , roll_Jensen_2D , beta_TRF

# buffer function takes care of this error:
ValueError: A value (...) in
x_new is below the interpolation range's minimum value (...)
231
def buffer(beta_sorted , H_sorted , eps=1e-6):
232
beta_sorted = np.asarray(beta_sorted , float).ravel()
233
H_sorted
= np.asarray(H_sorted)
234
235
beta_buf = np.concatenate(([beta_sorted[0] - eps], beta_sorted , [
beta_sorted[-1] + eps]))
236
H_buf
= np.concatenate([H_sorted[:, [0]], H_sorted , H_sorted[:,
[-1]]], axis=1)
237
return beta_buf , H_buf

def zero_initial_decrease_until(f, S, fmax=0.03):
241
f = np.asarray(f, float)
242
S = np.asarray(S, float).copy()
243
n = len(f)
244
if n < 2:
245
return S
246
last = -1
247
for i in range(n-1):
248
if f[i+1] > float(fmax):
249
break
250
if S[i+1] <= S[i]:
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
121
==PAGE:128==
251
last = i+1
252
else:
253
break
254
if last >= 0:
255
S[:last+1] = 0.0
256
S[S < 0.0] = 0.0
257
return S

def _smooth_for_plot(y, df, width_hz=0.03):
264
n = max(1, int(round(width_hz / df)))
265
if n % 2 == 0:
266
n += 1
# making sure the number is odd so the average is not biased
267
return uniform_filter1d(y, size=n, mode="nearest")

def accel_to_motion_psd(f, Sa, eps=0.02):
271

272

273

# guard near 0 Hz
274
return Sa / ((2 * np.pi * fe) ** 4)

def load_rao_dat(path):
43
rao = np.loadtxt(path, comments='%')
44
f = rao[:, 0]
45
46
block_cut = [0]
47
for i in range(1, len(f)):
48
if f[i] < f[i-1]:
49
block_cut.append(i)
50
block_cut.append(len(f))
51
52
number_of_blocks = len(block_cut) - 1
53
delta_beta = 360/number_of_blocks
54
beta = (np.arange(number_of_blocks) * delta_beta)
# beta-from:
0=head, 180=following
55
freq = rao[block_cut[0]:block_cut[1], 0]
56
57
def block(col):
58
return np.stack([rao[block_cut[k]:block_cut[k+1], col] for k in
range(number_of_blocks)], axis=0)
59
60
return xr.Dataset(
61
data_vars=dict(
62
heave_re=(("beta","freq"), block(5)),
63
heave_im=(("beta","freq"), block(6)),
64
roll_re =(("beta","freq"), np.deg2rad(block(7))),
65
roll_im =(("beta","freq"), np.deg2rad(block(8))),
66
pitch_re=(("beta","freq"), np.deg2rad(block(9))),
67
pitch_im=(("beta","freq"), np.deg2rad(block(10))),
68
),
69
coords=dict(beta=beta.astype(float), freq=freq.astype(float)),
70
)
71

def speed_key(u_knots):
73
return int(u_knots)

76
return load_rao_dat(RAO_files[speed_key_int])

def preparing_RAO_inputs_for_netsse(freq0, mu_from_deg , ws0_per_rad , ds_rao ,
dof, target_freq_Hz=None):
80
mu = np.asarray(mu_from_deg , float).ravel()
# mu is already
“”from
81
if mu.size >= 2 and np.isclose((mu[-1]-mu[0]) % 360.0, 0.0, atol=1e-6):
82
mu_safe_end = mu[:-1]
83
else:
84
mu_safe_end = mu
85
86
f_original = np.asarray(freq0, float).ravel()
87
f_evenly_spaced = (target_freq_Hz if target_freq_Hz is not None
88
else np.linspace(f_original.min(), f_original.max(),
len(f_original)))
89
90
wave_spec_original = np.asarray(ws0_per_rad , float)
91
if wave_spec_original.shape[0] != f_original.size:
92
wave_spec_original = wave_spec_original.T
93
94
wave_spec_interp = np.empty((len(f_evenly_spaced), wave_spec_original.
shape[1]), float)
95
for k in range(wave_spec_original.shape[1]):
96
wave_spec_interp[:, k] = np.interp(f_evenly_spaced , f_original ,
wave_spec_original[:, k], left=0.0, right=0.0)
97
98
freq_RAO = ds_rao["freq"].values
99
beta_RAO = ds_rao["beta"].values
# beta is “”from
100
Re = ds_rao[f"{dof}_re"].values
101
Im = ds_rao[f"{dof}_im"].values
102
RAO_interpolated = np.empty((len(f_evenly_spaced), len(beta_RAO)),
complex)
103
for b in range(len(beta_RAO)):
104
trf_Re = np.interp(f_evenly_spaced , freq_RAO , Re[b, :], left=0.0,
right=0.0)
105
trf_Im = np.interp(f_evenly_spaced , freq_RAO , Im[b, :], left=0.0,
right=0.0)
106
RAO_interpolated[:, b] = trf_Re + 1j * trf_Im
107
108
return f_evenly_spaced , mu_safe_end , wave_spec_interp , beta_RAO ,
RAO_interpolated

# --- Jensen Transfer Functions --------------------------------
112
def relative_angle(ship_heading_deg , wave_dir_from_deg):
113
# both in “”from
114
return (wave_dir_from_deg - ship_heading_deg) % 360.0
115
116
def Jensen_transfer_functions(beta_deg , omega, U, ship_params):
117
g
= ship_params["g"];
L
= ship_params["L"];
B
= ship_params["B
"];
T
= ship_params["T"]
118
Cb
= ship_params["Cb"];
Cwp = ship_params["Cwp"]; delta = ship_params[
"delta"]; mu_damp = ship_params["mu_damp"]
119
GMt = ship_params["GMt"]; rho
= ship_params["rho"]
120
121
beta_rad = np.radians(beta_deg)
122
B0 = B / Cb
123
C_Tn = 0.373 + 0.023*(B0/T) - 0.043*(L/100)
124
Tn = 2*C_Tn*B0/np.sqrt(GMt)
125
126
k = (omega**2) / g
127
k = np.where(k < 1e-100, 1e-100, k)
128
129
omega_e = omega - k*U*np.cos(beta_rad)
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
127
==PAGE:134==
130
Fn = U / np.sqrt(g*L)
131
alpha = 1 - Fn*np.sqrt(k*L)*np.cos(beta_rad)
132
133
A = 2*np.sin(0.5*k*B*alpha**2) * np.exp(-k*T*alpha**2)
134
ke = np.abs(k*np.cos(beta_rad)); ke = np.where(ke < 1e-100, 1e-100, ke)
135
kappa = np.exp(-k*T)
136
ff = np.sqrt((1 - k*T)**2 + (A**2 / (k*B*alpha**3))**2)
137
138
F = np.abs(kappa*ff*(2/(ke*L))*np.sin(ke*L/2))
139
G = np.abs(kappa*ff*(24/(L*(ke*L)**2))*(np.sin(ke*L/2)-((ke*L/2)*np.cos(
ke*L/2))))
140
eta = 1/np.sqrt((1 - (2*k*T*alpha**2))**2 + (A**2/(k*B*alpha**2))**2)
141
142
heave = np.abs(eta*F)
143
pitch = np.abs(eta*G)
144
145
displ = L*T*B*rho
146
C44 = g*displ*GMt
147
gamma = np.clip((Cwp - delta)/(1 - delta), 1/6, 6)
148
B1 = gamma*(B / Cb)
149
A0 = (Cb*(B/Cb)*T)/(delta + gamma*(1 - delta))
150
A1 = gamma*A0
151
B0_T = B0/T
152
B1_T = max(B1/T, 1.0)
153
154
if 3 <= B0_T <= 6:
aBT = 0.256*B0_T - 0.286; bBT = -0.11*B0_T - 2.55;
dBT = 0.033*B0_T - 1.419
155
elif 1 <= B0_T < 3:
aBT = -3.94*B0_T + 13.69;
bBT = -2.12*B0_T - 1.89;
dBT = 1.16*B0_T - 7.97
156
else:
aBT = bBT = dBT = 0.0
157
158
if 3 <= B1_T <= 6:
aBT1 = 0.256*B1_T - 0.286; bBT1 = -0.11*B1_T -
2.55; dBT1 = 0.033*B1_T - 1.419
159
elif 1 <= B1_T < 3:
aBT1 = -3.94*B1_T + 13.69;
bBT1 = -2.12*B1_T -
1.89; dBT1 = 1.16*B1_T - 7.97
160
else:
aBT1 = bBT1 = dBT1 = 0.0
161
162
omega_e = np.where(omega_e < 1e-10, 1e-10, omega_e)
163
b440 = aBT
* np.exp(bBT
* omega_e**(-1.3)) * omega_e**dBT
* (rho*A0*(
B/Cb)**2) * (1/np.sqrt((B/Cb)/(2*9.81)))
164
b440 = np.where(b440 < 1e-100, 1e-100, b440)
165
b441 = aBT1 * np.exp(bBT1 * omega_e**(-1.3)) * omega_e**dBT1 * (rho*A1*
B1**2) * (1/np.sqrt(B1/(2*9.81)))
166
kappab = np.sqrt(b441/b440)
167
B44 = L*b440*(delta + (kappab**2)*(1 - delta))
168
B44_star = (C44*Tn)/np.pi
169
B44_total = B44 + mu_damp*B44_star
170
171
M_real = np.abs(np.sin(beta_rad)) * np.sqrt((rho*9.81**2)/omega_e) * (2/
ke) * np.sqrt(b440) * (
172
(np.sin(0.5*delta*L*ke)**2 + kappab**2*np.sin(0.5*(1 - delta)*L*ke)
**2 +
173
2*kappab*np.sin(0.5*delta*L*ke)*np.sin(0.5*(1 - delta)*L*ke)*np.cos
(0.5*L*ke)))**0.5
174
roll = M_real / np.sqrt(((-omega_e**2*(Tn/(2*np.pi))**2 + 1)**2 * C44
**2) + (omega_e**2 * B44_total**2))
175
176
return heave.real, pitch.real, roll.real
177

def Jensen_transfer_functions_2D(mu_from_deg , omega, U, ship_params ,
ship_heading_deg):
179
nmu, nfreq = len(mu_from_deg), len(omega)
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
128
==PAGE:135==
180
beta_TRF = np.empty(nmu, dtype=float)
181
heave_Jensen_2D = np.zeros((nfreq, nmu), float)
182
pitch_Jensen_2D = np.zeros((nfreq, nmu), float)
183
roll_Jensen_2D
= np.zeros((nfreq, nmu), float)
184
for j, mu_deg in enumerate(mu_from_deg):
# mu-from
185
beta = relative_angle(ship_heading_deg , mu_deg)
# beta-from
186
beta = beta % 360.0
187
beta_TRF[j] = beta
188
h, p, r = Jensen_transfer_functions(beta, omega, U, ship_params)
189
heave_Jensen_2D[:, j] = h; pitch_Jensen_2D[:, j] = p; roll_Jensen_2D
[:, j] = r
190
return heave_Jensen_2D , pitch_Jensen_2D , roll_Jensen_2D , beta_TRF

def preparing_Jensen_inputs_for_netsse(freq0, mu_from_deg , ws0_per_rad , U_ms
, psi_deg , ship_params , target_freq_Hz=None):
193
mu = np.asarray(mu_from_deg , float).ravel() % 360.0
# ￿-from
194
if mu.size >= 2 and np.isclose((mu[-1] - mu[0]) % 360.0, 0.0, atol=1e-6)
:
195
mu = mu[:-1]
196
mu_ascending = np.argsort(mu)
197
mu_safe = mu[mu_ascending]
198
199
f_original = np.asarray(freq0, float).ravel()
200
f_evenly_spaced = (target_freq_Hz if target_freq_Hz is not None
201
else np.linspace(f_original.min(), f_original.max(),
len(f_original)))
202
203
wave_spec_original = np.asarray(ws0_per_rad , float)[:, mu_ascending]
204
wave_spec_interp = np.empty((len(f_evenly_spaced), wave_spec_original.
shape[1]), float)
205
for k in range(wave_spec_original.shape[1]):
206
wave_spec_interp[:, k] = np.interp(f_evenly_spaced , f_original ,
wave_spec_original[:, k], left=0.0, right=0.0)
207
208
omega = 2 * np.pi * f_evenly_spaced
209
heave_Jensen_2D_asc , pitch_Jensen_2D_asc , roll_Jensen_2D_asc , beta_TRF =
Jensen_transfer_functions_2D(mu_safe , omega, U_ms, ship_params ,
psi_deg)
210
211
beta = np.mod(beta_TRF , 360.0)
# beta is from
212
beta_ascending = np.argsort(beta)
213
beta_safe = beta[beta_ascending]
214
return (f_evenly_spaced , mu_safe , wave_spec_interp , beta_safe ,
215
heave_Jensen_2D_asc[:, beta_ascending],
216
pitch_Jensen_2D_asc[:, beta_ascending],
217
roll_Jensen_2D_asc[:, beta_ascending])

def buffer(beta_sorted , H_sorted , eps=1e-6):
220
beta_sorted = np.asarray(beta_sorted , float).ravel()
221
H_sorted
= np.asarray(H_sorted)
222
beta_buf = np.concatenate(([beta_sorted[0] - eps], beta_sorted , [
beta_sorted[-1] + eps]))
223
H_buf
= np.concatenate([H_sorted[:, [0]], H_sorted , H_sorted[:,
[-1]]], axis=1)
224
return beta_buf , H_buf

# smoothing (weighted average)
332
def _smooth_for_plot(y, df, width_hz=0.03):
333
n = max(1, int(round(width_hz / df)))
334
if n % 2 == 0:
335
n += 1
# making sure the number is odd so the average is not biased
336
return uniform_filter1d(y, size=n, mode="nearest")
337
338
A_heave_Jensen_plot = _smooth_for_plot(A_heave_Jensen , df_enc)

= _smooth_for_plot(A_heave_RAO ,
df_enc)

df_enc)

df_enc)

df_enc)

def accel_to_motion_psd(f: np.ndarray , Sa: np.ndarray , eps: float =
ACC_EPS_HZ) -> np.ndarray:
46
47
f = np.asarray(f, float).ravel()
48
Sa = np.asarray(Sa, float).ravel()
49
fe = np.maximum(f, float(eps))
50
Sm = Sa / ((2*np.pi*fe)**4)
51
Sm[~np.isfinite(Sm)] = 0.0
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
133
==PAGE:140==
52
Sm[Sm < 0.0] = 0.0
53
return Sm

55
def zero_initial_decrease_until(f: np.ndarray , S: np.ndarray , fmax: float =
0.03) -> np.ndarray:
56
57
f = np.asarray(f, float)
58
S = np.asarray(S, float).copy()
59
if f.size < 2:
60
return S
61
last = -1
62
for i in range(f.size - 1):
63
if f[i + 1] > float(fmax):
64
break
65
if S[i + 1] <= S[i]:
66
last = i + 1
67
else:
68
break
69
if last >= 0:
70
S[: last + 1] = 0.0
71
S[S < 0.0] = 0.0
72
return S
73
74
def tp_from_psd(f: np.ndarray , S: np.ndarray , smooth_Tp: bool = True) ->
float:
75
76
f = np.asarray(f, float).ravel()
77
S = np.asarray(S, float).ravel()
78
if f.size == 0:
79
return np.nan
80
Ssafe = np.where(np.isfinite(S), S, -np.inf)
81
I = int(np.argmax(Ssafe))
82
if (not smooth_Tp) or I < 2 or I > f.size - 3:
83
fp = f[I]
84
return 1.0 / fp if (np.isfinite(fp) and fp > 0) else np.nan
85
# local cubic fit for refinement
86
x = f[I-2:I+3].astype(float)
87
y = S[I-2:I+3].astype(float)
88
if not (np.all(np.isfinite(x)) and np.all(np.isfinite(y))):
89
fp = f[I]
90
return 1.0 / fp if (np.isfinite(fp) and fp > 0) else np.nan
91
z = np.polyfit(x, y, 3)
92
p = np.poly1d(z)
93
xd = np.linspace(x[0], x[-1], 200)
94
yd = p(xd)
95
fp = float(xd[int(np.argmax(yd))])
96
return 1.0 / fp if (np.isfinite(fp) and fp > 0) else np.nan
97
98
def spectral_moments(f: np.ndarray , S: np.ndarray) -> Tuple[float, float,
float]:
99
100
f = np.asarray(f, float).ravel()
101
S = np.asarray(S, float).ravel()
102
if f.size < 2 or S.size < 2:
103
return (np.nan, np.nan, np.nan)
104
m0 = np.trapezoid(S, f)
105
m1 = np.trapezoid(f * S, f)
106
m2 = np.trapezoid((f**2) * S, f)
107
return (m0, m1, m2)
108
109
def t_periods_from_moments(m0: float, m1: float, m2: float) -> Tuple[float,
float]:
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
134
==PAGE:141==
110
111
Tz = np.sqrt(m0 / m2) if (np.isfinite(m0) and np.isfinite(m2) and m2 >
0) else np.nan
112
Tm01 = (m0 / m1) if (np.isfinite(m0) and np.isfinite(m1) and m1 > 0)
else np.nan
113
return (float(Tz), float(Tm01))
114
115
def stats_from_motion_psd_fine(
116
f: np.ndarray , S: np.ndarray , df: float = DF_STATS_HZ , fmin: float =
F_MIN_HZ , fmax: float = F_MAX_HZ
117
) -> Tuple[float, float, float, float]:
118
119
f = np.asarray(f, float).ravel()
120
S = np.asarray(S, float).ravel()
121
122
# mask to [fmin, fmax]
123
m = np.isfinite(f) & np.isfinite(S) & (f >= float(fmin)) & (f <= float(
fmax))
124
if np.count_nonzero(m) < 2:
125
return (np.nan, np.nan, np.nan, np.nan)
126
127
f_use, S_use = f[m], S[m]
128
fmin_u , fmax_u = float(f_use.min()), float(f_use.max())
129
if not (np.isfinite(fmin_u) and np.isfinite(fmax_u) and fmax_u > fmin_u)
:
130
return (np.nan, np.nan, np.nan, np.nan)
131
132
# fine grid
133
nsteps = int(np.floor((fmax_u - fmin_u) / float(df))) + 1
134
f_fine = fmin_u + np.arange(nsteps + 1) * float(df)
135
f_fine = f_fine[(f_fine >= fmin_u - 1e-12) & (f_fine <= fmax_u + 1e-12)]
136
137
# linear interp to fine grid
138
S_fine = np.interp(f_fine, f_use, S_use, left=0.0, right=0.0)
139
S_fine[~np.isfinite(S_fine)] = 0.0
140
S_fine[S_fine < 0.0] = 0.0
141
142
# moments & periods
143
m0, m1, m2 = spectral_moments(f_fine, S_fine)
144
Hm0_2 = 2.0 * np.sqrt(max(m0, 0.0)) if np.isfinite(m0) else np.nan
145
Tz, Tm01 = t_periods_from_moments(m0, m1, m2)
146
147
# Tp via refined peak
148
Tp = tp_from_psd(f_fine, S_fine, smooth_Tp=True)
149
150
return float(Hm0_2), float(Tz), float(Tm01), float(Tp)
151
152
def build_edges(x: np.ndarray , bin_width: float, nbins: int) -> np.ndarray:
153
x = np.asarray(x, float); x = x[np.isfinite(x)]
154
if x.size == 0:
155
return np.array([0.0, 1.0])
156
lo, hi = float(np.min(x)), float(np.max(x))
157
if not np.isfinite(lo) or not np.isfinite(hi) or hi <= lo:
158
lo, hi = 0.0, 1.0
159
if bin_width and bin_width > 0:
160
start = np.floor(lo / bin_width) * bin_width
161
end
= np.ceil(hi / bin_width) * bin_width
162
return np.arange(start, end + 0.5 * bin_width , bin_width)
163
return np.linspace(lo, hi, int(nbins) + 1)
164

def histogram_table(x: np.ndarray , edges: np.ndarray) -> pd.DataFrame:
166
x = np.asarray(x, float); x = x[np.isfinite(x)]
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
135
==PAGE:142==
167
counts , edges = np.histogram(x, bins=edges)
168
df = pd.DataFrame({"bin_left": edges[:-1], "bin_right": edges[1:], "
count": counts})
169
df["cum_count"] = df["count"].cumsum()
170
total = int(df["count"].sum())
171
df["cum_frac"]
= df["cum_count"] / total if total > 0 else 0.0
172
return df

def get_freq_from_npz(z: np.lib.npyio.NpzFile) -> np.ndarray:
42
43
for key in ["f_heave", "f_pitch", "f_roll", "fe_shared_hz", "freq_hz", "
fe_hz", "f", "freq"]:
44
if key in z:
45
f = np.asarray(z[key], float).ravel()
46
if f.size >= 2 and np.all(np.isfinite(f)) and np.max(f) > 0:
47
return f
48
raise KeyError(
49
"No frequency vector found in GT NPZ "
50
"(looked for f_heave/f_pitch/f_roll/fe_shared_hz/freq_hz/fe_hz/f/
freq)."
51

def tp_from_psd(f: np.ndarray , S: np.ndarray , smooth_Tp: bool = True) ->
float:
54
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
142
==PAGE:149==
55
f = np.asarray(f, float).ravel()
56
S = np.asarray(S, float).ravel()
57
if f.size == 0:
58
return np.nan
59
Ssafe = np.where(np.isfinite(S), S, -np.inf)
60
I = int(np.argmax(Ssafe))
61
if (not smooth_Tp) or I < 2 or I > f.size - 3:
62
fp = f[I]
63
return 1.0 / fp if (np.isfinite(fp) and fp > 0) else np.nan
64
x = f[I-2:I+3].astype(float)
65
y = S[I-2:I+3].astype(float)
66
if not (np.all(np.isfinite(x)) and np.all(np.isfinite(y))):
67
fp = f[I]
68
return 1.0 / fp if (np.isfinite(fp) and fp > 0) else np.nan
69
z = np.polyfit(x, y, 3)
70
p = np.poly1d(z)
71
xd = np.linspace(x[0], x[-1], 200)
72
yd = p(xd)
73
fp = float(xd[int(np.argmax(yd))])
74
return 1.0 / fp if (np.isfinite(fp) and fp > 0) else np.nan
75
76
def stats_from_acc_psd_fine(
77
f: np.ndarray , S: np.ndarray , df: float = 0.001, fmax: float = 0.3
78
) -> Tuple[float, float, float, float]:
79
80
f = np.asarray(f, float).ravel()
81
S = np.asarray(S, float).ravel()
82
83
m = np.isfinite(f) & np.isfinite(S) & (f > 0) & (f <= float(fmax))
84
if np.count_nonzero(m) < 2:
85
return (np.nan, np.nan, np.nan, np.nan)
86
87
f_use, S_use = f[m], S[m]
88
fmin_u , fmax_u = float(f_use.min()), float(f_use.max())
89
if not (np.isfinite(fmin_u) and np.isfinite(fmax_u) and fmax_u > fmin_u)
:
90
return (np.nan, np.nan, np.nan, np.nan)
91
92
# fine, evenly spaced grid
93
nsteps = int(np.floor((fmax_u - fmin_u) / float(df))) + 1
94
f_fine = fmin_u + np.arange(nsteps + 1) * float(df)
95
f_fine = f_fine[(f_fine >= fmin_u - 1e-12) & (f_fine <= fmax_u + 1e-12)]
96
97
# interpolate PSD onto fine grid
98
S_fine = np.interp(f_fine, f_use, S_use, left=0.0, right=0.0)
99
100
# moments + Tp
101
m0 = np.trapezoid(S_fine, f_fine)
102
m1 = np.trapezoid(f_fine * S_fine , f_fine)
103
m2 = np.trapezoid((f_fine**2) * S_fine , f_fine)
104
Hm0_2 = 2.0 * np.sqrt(max(m0, 0.0)) if np.isfinite(m0) else np.nan
105
Tz
= np.sqrt(m0/m2) if (m2 > 0 and np.isfinite(m0) and np.isfinite(m2
)) else np.nan
106
Tm01
= (m0/m1)
if (m1 > 0 and np.isfinite(m0) and np.isfinite(m1
)) else np.nan
107
Tp
= tp_from_psd(f_fine , S_fine, smooth_Tp=True)
108
109
return Hm0_2, Tz, Tm01, Tp
110
111
def build_edges(x: np.ndarray , bin_width: float, nbins: int) -> np.ndarray:
112
x = np.asarray(x, float); x = x[np.isfinite(x)]
113
if x.size == 0:
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
143
==PAGE:150==
114
return np.array([0.0, 1.0])
115
lo, hi = float(np.min(x)), float(np.max(x))
116
if not np.isfinite(lo) or not np.isfinite(hi) or hi <= lo:
117
lo, hi = 0.0, 1.0
118
if bin_width and bin_width > 0:
119
start = np.floor(lo / bin_width) * bin_width
120
end
= np.ceil(hi / bin_width) * bin_width
121
return np.arange(start, end + 0.5 * bin_width , bin_width)
122
return np.linspace(lo, hi, int(nbins) + 1)

def histogram_table(x: np.ndarray , edges: np.ndarray) -> pd.DataFrame:
125
x = np.asarray(x, float); x = x[np.isfinite(x)]
126
counts , edges = np.histogram(x, bins=edges)
127
df = pd.DataFrame({"bin_left": edges[:-1], "bin_right": edges[1:], "
count": counts})
128
df["cum_count"] = df["count"].cumsum()
129
total = int(df["count"].sum())
130
df["cum_frac"]
= df["cum_count"] / total if total > 0 else 0.0
131
return df

def plot_hist(x: np.ndarray , title: str, xlabel: str, out_png: str, edges:
np.ndarray) -> None:
134
x = np.asarray(x, float); x = x[np.isfinite(x)]
135
if x.size == 0:
136
print(f"[warn] nothing to plot: {out_png}"); return
137
plt.figure(figsize=FIGSIZE)
138
plt.hist(x, bins=edges, edgecolor="black", linewidth=0.8, alpha=0.7)
139
q50, q75, q90 = np.quantile(x, [0.5, 0.75, 0.90])
140
for q, ls, lw, lab in [(q50, "-", 2.0, "Q50"), (q75, "--", 1.5, "Q75"),
(q90, ":", 1.5, "Q90")]:
141
plt.axvline(q, linestyle=ls, linewidth=lw, label=f"{lab}={q:.3g}")
142
plt.xlabel(xlabel); plt.ylabel("count"); plt.title(title)
143
plt.grid(alpha=0.25); plt.legend()
144
plt.tight_layout(); plt.savefig(out_png , dpi=DPI); plt.close()

print(f"saved: {out_png}")

def load_rao_dat(path):

95

96

# 0..350
103

104

105
return np.stack([rao[cuts[k]:cuts[k+1], col] for k in range(nb)],
axis=0)
106
return xr.Dataset(
107

heave_re=(("beta","freq"), block(5)),
109
heave_im=(("beta","freq"), block(6)),

roll_re =(("beta","freq"), np.deg2rad(block(7))),

pitch_re=(("beta","freq"), np.deg2rad(block(9))),

),

coords=dict(beta=beta.astype(float), freq=freq.astype(float)),

# -------- Jensen TFs
126
def Jensen_transfer_functions(beta_deg , omega, U, ship):
127
g,L,B,T = ship["g"], ship["L"], ship["B"], ship["T"]
128
Cb,Cwp,delta,mu = ship["Cb"], ship["Cwp"], ship["delta"], ship["mu"]
129
GMt,rho = ship["GMt"], ship["rho"]
130
br = np.radians(beta_deg)
131
B0 = B/Cb
132
C_Tn = 0.373 + 0.023*(B0/T) - 0.043*(L/100)
133
Tn = 2*C_Tn*B0/np.sqrt(GMt)
134
k = (omega**2)/g
135
k = np.where(k < 1e-100, 1e-100, k)
136
omega_e = omega - k*U*np.cos(br)
137
Fn = U/np.sqrt(g*L)
138
alpha = 1 - Fn*np.sqrt(k*L)*np.cos(br)
139
A = 2*np.sin(0.5*k*B*alpha**2)*np.exp(-k*T*alpha**2)
140
ke = np.abs(k*np.cos(br)); ke = np.where(ke < 1e-100, 1e-100, ke)
141
kappa = np.exp(-k*T)
142
ff = np.sqrt((1 - k*T)**2 + (A**2/(k*B*alpha**3))**2)
143
F = np.abs(kappa*ff*(2/(ke*L))*np.sin(ke*L/2))
144
G = np.abs(kappa*ff*(24/(L*(ke*L)**2))*(np.sin(ke*L/2)-((ke*L/2)*np.cos(
ke*L/2))))
145
eta = 1/np.sqrt((1 - (2*k*T*alpha**2))**2 + (A**2/(k*B*alpha**2))**2)
146
heave = np.abs(eta*F)
147
pitch = np.abs(eta*G)
148
displ = L*T*B*rho
149
C44 = g*displ*GMt
150
gamma = np.clip((Cwp - delta)/(1 - delta), 1/6, 6)
151
B1 = gamma*(B/Cb)
152
A0 = (Cb*(B/Cb)*T)/(delta + gamma*(1 - delta))
153
A1 = gamma*A0
154
B0_T = (B/Cb)/T
155
B1_T = max(B1/T, 1.0)
156
def coef(BT):
157
if 3 <= BT <= 6: return 0.256*BT - 0.286, -0.11*BT - 2.55, 0.033*BT
- 1.419
158
if 1 <= BT < 3:
return -3.94*BT + 13.69, -2.12*BT - 1.89, 1.16*BT -
7.97
159
return 0.0, 0.0, 0.0
160
aBT,bBT,dBT
= coef(B0_T)
161
aBT1,bBT1,dBT1 = coef(B1_T)
162
omega_e = np.where(omega_e < 1e-10, 1e-10, omega_e).astype(np.complex128
)
163
b440 = aBT * np.exp(bBT * omega_e**(-1.3)) * omega_e**dBT
164
b440 = np.where(b440 < 1e-100, 1e-100, b440)
165
b441 = aBT1* np.exp(bBT1* omega_e**(-1.3)) * omega_e**dBT1
166
kappab = np.sqrt(b441/b440)
167
B44 = L*b440*(delta + (kappab**2)*(1 - delta))
168
B44_star
= (C44*Tn)/np.pi
169
B44_total = B44 + mu * B44_star
170
M_real = np.abs(np.sin(br)) * np.sqrt((rho*9.81**2)/omega_e) * (2/ke) *
np.sqrt(b440) * (
171
(np.sin(0.5*delta*L*ke)**2 + kappab**2*np.sin(0.5*(1 - delta)*L*ke)
**2 +
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
151
==PAGE:158==
172
2*kappab*np.sin(0.5*delta*L*ke)*np.sin(0.5*(1 - delta)*L*ke)*np.cos
(0.5*L*ke)))**0.5
173
roll = M_real / np.sqrt(((-omega_e**2*(Tn/(2*np.pi))**2 + 1)**2 * C44
**2) + (omega_e**2 * B44_total**2))
174
return heave.real, pitch.real, roll.real
175
176
def Jensen_transfer_functions_2D(mu_from_deg , omega, U, ship, psi_deg):
177
nmu, nfreq = len(mu_from_deg), len(omega)
178
beta_list = np.empty(nmu, float)
179
Hh = np.zeros((nfreq, nmu)); Hp = np.zeros((nfreq, nmu)); Hr = np.zeros
((nfreq, nmu))
180
for j, mu_f in enumerate(mu_from_deg):
181
beta = (mu_f - psi_deg) % 360; beta_list[j] = beta
182
h, p, r = Jensen_transfer_functions(beta, omega, U, ship)
183
Hh[:, j] = h; Hp[:, j] = p; Hr[:, j] = r
184
return Hh, Hp, Hr, beta_list
185
186
def preparing_RAO_inputs_for_netsse(freq0, mu_from_deg , ws0, ds_rao , dof):
187
mu = np.asarray(mu_from_deg , float).ravel()
188
mu = mu[:-1] if (mu.size >=2 and np.isclose((mu[-1]-mu[0])%360.0, 0.0,
atol=1e-6)) else mu
189
f0 = np.asarray(freq0, float).ravel()
190
f_even = np.linspace(f0.min(), f0.max(), len(f0))
191
ws = np.asarray(ws0, float)
192
if ws.shape[0] != f0.size: ws = ws.T
193
ws_i = np.empty((len(f_even), ws.shape[1]), float)
194
for k in range(ws.shape[1]):
195
ws_i[:, k] = np.interp(f_even, f0, ws[:, k], left=0.0, right=0.0)
196
freq_RAO = ds_rao["freq"].values; beta_RAO = ds_rao["beta"].values
197
Re = ds_rao[f"{dof}_re"].values; Im = ds_rao[f"{dof}_im"].values
198
H = np.empty((len(f_even), len(beta_RAO)), complex)
199
for b in range(len(beta_RAO)):
200
tr = np.interp(f_even, freq_RAO , Re[b, :], left=0.0, right=0.0)
201
ti = np.interp(f_even, freq_RAO , Im[b, :], left=0.0, right=0.0)
202
H[:, b] = tr + 1j*ti
203
return f_even, mu, ws_i, beta_RAO , H
204
205
def preparing_Jensen_inputs_for_netsse(freq0, mu_from_deg , ws0, U_ms,
psi_deg , ship):
206
mu = (np.asarray(mu_from_deg , float).ravel()) % 360
207
if mu.size >=2 and np.isclose((mu[-1]-mu[0])%360.0, 0.0, atol=1e-6): mu =
mu[:-1]
208
idx = np.argsort(mu); mu_safe = mu[idx]
209
f0 = np.asarray(freq0, float).ravel()
210
f_even = np.linspace(f0.min(), f0.max(), len(f0))
211
ws = np.asarray(ws0, float)[:, idx]
212
ws_i = np.empty((len(f_even), ws.shape[1]), float)
213
for k in range(ws.shape[1]):
214
ws_i[:, k] = np.interp(f_even, f0, ws[:, k], left=0.0, right=0.0)
215
omega = 2*np.pi*f_even
216
Hh, Hp, Hr, beta = Jensen_transfer_functions_2D(mu_safe , omega, U_ms,
ship, psi_deg)
217
beta = np.mod(beta, 360.0); ib = np.argsort(beta)
218
return f_even, mu_safe , ws_i, beta[ib], Hh[:, ib], Hp[:, ib], Hr[:, ib]
219
220
def buffer(beta_sorted , H_sorted , eps=1e-6):
221
beta_sorted = np.asarray(beta_sorted , float).ravel()
222
H_sorted
= np.asarray(H_sorted)
223
beta_buf = np.concatenate(([beta_sorted[0]-eps], beta_sorted , [
beta_sorted[-1]+eps]))
224
H_buf
= np.concatenate([H_sorted[:, [0]], H_sorted , H_sorted[:,
[-1]]], axis=1)
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
152
==PAGE:159==
225
return beta_buf , H_buf

ship_params = dict(g=9.81, L=232.0, B=32.2, T=10.78, Cb=0.685, Cwp=0.713,
delta=0.62, mu=0.4, GMt=1.15, rho=1025.0)

def align_to_grid(f_src, S_src, f_tgt):
230
f_src = np.asarray(f_src, float).ravel()
231
S_src = np.asarray(S_src, float).ravel()
232
return np.interp(f_tgt, f_src, S_src, left=0.0, right=0.0)
233
234
# -------- Metrics & stats (MOTION domain)
------------------------------------
235
def niae_band(f_Hz, est_psd , gt_psd , fmax):
236
f = np.asarray(f_Hz, float).ravel()
237
e = np.asarray(est_psd , float).ravel()
238
g = np.asarray(gt_psd,
float).ravel()
239
mask = (f > 0) & (f <= float(fmax))
240
if not np.any(mask): return np.nan
241
f, e, g = f[mask], e[mask], g[mask]
242
num = np.trapezoid(np.abs(e - g), f)
243
den = np.trapezoid(np.maximum(g, 0.0), f)
244
return np.nan if den <= 0 else (num/den)
245
246
def accel_psd_to_motion_psd(f, Sa, eps=0.02):
247
f = np.asarray(f, float).ravel()
248
Sa = np.asarray(Sa, float).ravel()
249
fe = np.maximum(f, float(eps))
250
Sm = Sa / ((2*np.pi*fe)**4)
251
Sm[~np.isfinite(Sm)] = 0.0
252
return Sm
253
254
def tp_from_psd(f, S, smooth_Tp=True):
255
f = np.asarray(f, float).ravel()
256
S = np.asarray(S, float).ravel()
257
if f.size == 0:
258
return np.nan
259
Ssafe = np.where(np.isfinite(S), S, -np.inf)
260
I = int(np.argmax(Ssafe))
261
if (not smooth_Tp) or I < 2 or I > f.size -3:
262
fp = f[I]
263
return 1.0/fp if (np.isfinite(fp) and fp > 0) else np.nan
264
x = f[I-2:I+3].astype(float); y = S[I-2:I+3].astype(float)
265
if not (np.all(np.isfinite(x)) and np.all(np.isfinite(y))):
266
fp = f[I]; return 1.0/fp if (np.isfinite(fp) and fp > 0) else np.nan
267
z = np.polyfit(x, y, 3); p = np.poly1d(z)
268
xd = np.linspace(x[0], x[-1], 200); yd = p(xd)
269
fp = float(xd[int(np.argmax(yd))])
270
return 1.0/fp if (np.isfinite(fp) and fp > 0) else np.nan
271
272
def stats_from_motion_psd_fine_band(f, S, df=0.001, fmin=1/26.0, fmax=0.3):
273
"""
274
Stats on a fine, evenly -spaced grid inside [fmin, fmax] (Hz).
275
Using cubic-peak-refined Tp so Tp ￿[1/fmax, 1/fmin] by construction.
276
"""
277
f = np.asarray(f, float).ravel()
278
S = np.asarray(S, float).ravel()
279
280
m = np.isfinite(f) & np.isfinite(S) & (f >= float(fmin)) & (f <= float(
fmax))
281
if np.count_nonzero(m) < 2:
282
return (np.nan, np.nan, np.nan, np.nan)
283
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
153
==PAGE:160==
284
f_use, S_use = f[m], S[m]
285
fmin_u , fmax_u = float(f_use.min()), float(f_use.max())
286
if not (np.isfinite(fmin_u) and np.isfinite(fmax_u) and fmax_u > fmin_u)
:
287
return (np.nan, np.nan, np.nan, np.nan)
288
289
nsteps = int(np.floor((fmax_u - fmin_u) / float(df))) + 1
290
f_fine = fmin_u + np.arange(nsteps + 1) * float(df)
291
f_fine = f_fine[(f_fine >= fmin_u - 1e-12) & (f_fine <= fmax_u + 1e-12)]
292
293
S_fine = np.interp(f_fine, f_use, S_use, left=0.0, right=0.0)
294
295
m0 = np.trapezoid(S_fine, f_fine)
296
m1 = np.trapezoid(f_fine * S_fine , f_fine)
297
m2 = np.trapezoid((f_fine**2) * S_fine , f_fine)
298
Hm0_2 = 2.0 * np.sqrt(max(m0, 0.0)) if np.isfinite(m0) else np.nan
299
Tz
= np.sqrt(m0/m2) if (m2 > 0 and np.isfinite(m0) and np.isfinite(m2
)) else np.nan
300
Tm01
= (m0/m1)
if (m1 > 0 and np.isfinite(m0) and np.isfinite(m1
)) else np.nan
301
Tp
= tp_from_psd(f_fine , S_fine, smooth_Tp=True)
302
return Hm0_2, Tz, Tm01, Tp
303

def scatter_with_identity(ax, x, y, panel_title , xlabel , ylabel , xlim):
355
try: ax.set_box_aspect(1)
356
except Exception: pass
357
lo, hi = xlim
358

359

360
ax.set_xlim(lo, hi); ax.set_ylim(lo, hi)
361
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
362
ax.set_title(panel_title)
363
ax.set_xlabel(xlabel); ax.set_ylabel(ylabel)
364
ax.grid(alpha=0.2)
365
366
if x.size >= 2 and np.nanstd(x) > 0 and np.nanstd(y) > 0:
367
m, b = np.polyfit(x, y, 1)
368
r = np.corrcoef(x, y)[0, 1]
369
r2 = r*r if np.isfinite(r) else np.nan
370
rmse = np.sqrt(np.mean((y - x)**2))
371
mae
= np.mean(np.abs(y - x))
372
ax.plot([lo, hi], [m*lo+b, m*hi+b], linewidth=1)
373
txt = [f"y = {m:.3g} x + {b:.3g}", f"R² = {r2:.3g}", f"RMSE = {rmse
:.3g}", f"MAE = {mae:.3g}", f"n = {x.size}"]
374
else:
375
txt = ["insufficient data"]
376
bbox = dict(boxstyle="round,pad=0.3", facecolor="white", edgecolor="
black", linewidth=0.8, alpha=0.9)
377
ax.text(0.98, 0.98, "\n".join(txt), transform=ax.transAxes , ha="right",
va="top", fontsize=9, bbox=bbox)

def zero_initial_decrease_until(f, S, fmax=0.03):
426
f = np.asarray(f, float)
427
S = np.asarray(S, float).copy()
428
n = len(f)
429
if n < 2:
430

for i in range(n - 1):
433
if f[i + 1] > float(fmax):
434
break

if S[i + 1] <= S[i]:
436
last = i + 1
437
else:
438
break
439
if last >= 0:
440
S[: last + 1] = 0.0
441
S[S < 0.0] = 0.0
442
return S

def load_rao_dat(path):

118

==PAGE:168==

# -------- Jensen TFs (B44_total = B44 + mu * B44_star)
148
def Jensen_transfer_functions(beta_deg , omega, U, ship):
149
g,L,B,T = ship["g"], ship["L"], ship["B"], ship["T"]
150
Cb,Cwp,delta,mu = ship["Cb"], ship["Cwp"], ship["delta"], ship["mu"]
151
GMt,rho = ship["GMt"], ship["rho"]
152
153
br = np.radians(beta_deg)
154
B0 = B/Cb
155
C_Tn = 0.373 + 0.023*(B0/T) - 0.043*(L/100)
156
Tn = 2*C_Tn*B0/np.sqrt(GMt)
157
158
k = (omega**2)/g
159
k = np.where(k < 1e-100, 1e-100, k)
160
161
omega_e = omega - k*U*np.cos(br)
162
Fn = U/np.sqrt(g*L)
163
alpha = 1 - Fn*np.sqrt(k*L)*np.cos(br)
164
165
A = 2*np.sin(0.5*k*B*alpha**2)*np.exp(-k*T*alpha**2)
166
ke = np.abs(k*np.cos(br)); ke = np.where(ke < 1e-100, 1e-100, ke)
167
kappa = np.exp(-k*T)
168
ff = np.sqrt((1 - k*T)**2 + (A**2/(k*B*alpha**3))**2)
169
170
F = np.abs(kappa*ff*(2/(ke*L))*np.sin(ke*L/2))
171
G = np.abs(kappa*ff*(24/(L*(ke*L)**2))*(np.sin(ke*L/2)-((ke*L/2)*np.cos(
ke*L/2))))
172
eta = 1/np.sqrt((1 - (2*k*T*alpha**2))**2 + (A**2/(k*B*alpha**2))**2)
173
174
heave = np.abs(eta*F)
175
pitch = np.abs(eta*G)
176
177
displ = L*T*B*rho
178
C44 = g*displ*GMt
179
gamma = np.clip((Cwp - delta)/(1 - delta), 1/6, 6)
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
162
==PAGE:169==
180
B1 = gamma*(B/Cb)
181
A0 = (Cb*(B/Cb)*T)/(delta + gamma*(1 - delta))
182
A1 = gamma*A0
183
B0_T = (B/Cb)/T
184
B1_T = max(B1/T, 1.0)
185
186
def coef(BT):
187
if 3 <= BT <= 6: return 0.256*BT - 0.286, -0.11*BT - 2.55, 0.033*BT
- 1.419
188
if 1 <= BT < 3:
return -3.94*BT + 13.69, -2.12*BT - 1.89, 1.16*BT -
7.97
189
return 0.0, 0.0, 0.0
190
aBT,bBT,dBT
= coef(B0_T)
191
aBT1,bBT1,dBT1 = coef(B1_T)
192
193
omega_e = np.where(omega_e < 1e-10, 1e-10, omega_e).astype(np.complex128
)
194
b440 = aBT * np.exp(bBT * omega_e**(-1.3)) * omega_e**dBT
* (rho*A0*(B/
Cb)**2) * (1/np.sqrt((B/Cb)/(2*9.81)))
195
b440 = np.where(b440 < 1e-100, 1e-100, b440)
196
b441 = aBT1* np.exp(bBT1* omega_e**(-1.3)) * omega_e**dBT1 * (rho*A1*B1
**2)
* (1/np.sqrt(B1/(2*9.81)))
197
kappab = np.sqrt(b441/b440)
198
B44 = L*b440*(delta + (kappab**2)*(1 - delta))
199
B44_star
= (C44*Tn)/np.pi
200
B44_total = B44 + mu * B44_star
201
202
M_real = np.abs(np.sin(br)) * np.sqrt((rho*9.81**2)/omega_e) * (2/ke) *
np.sqrt(b440) * (
203
(np.sin(0.5*delta*L*ke)**2 + kappab**2*np.sin(0.5*(1 - delta)*L*ke)
**2 +
204
2*kappab*np.sin(0.5*delta*L*ke)*np.sin(0.5*(1 - delta)*L*ke)*np.cos
(0.5*L*ke)))**0.5
205
roll = M_real / np.sqrt(((-omega_e**2*(Tn/(2*np.pi))**2 + 1)**2 * C44
**2) + (omega_e**2 * B44_total**2))
206
207
return heave.real, pitch.real, roll.real
208
209
def Jensen_transfer_functions_2D(mu_deg, omega, U, ship, psi_deg):
210
nmu, nfreq = len(mu_deg), len(omega)
211
beta_list = np.empty(nmu, float)
212
Hh = np.zeros((nfreq, nmu)); Hp = np.zeros((nfreq, nmu)); Hr = np.zeros
((nfreq, nmu))
213
for j, mu in enumerate(mu_deg):
214
beta = (mu - psi_deg) % 360; beta_list[j] = beta
215
h, p, r = Jensen_transfer_functions(beta, omega, U, ship)
216
Hh[:, j] = h; Hp[:, j] = p; Hr[:, j] = r
217
return Hh, Hp, Hr, beta_list

def preparing_RAO_inputs_for_netsse(freq0, mu_deg , ws0, ds_rao , dof):
220
mu = np.asarray(mu_deg, float).ravel()
221
mu = mu[:-1] if (mu.size >=2 and np.isclose((mu[-1]-mu[0])%360.0, 0.0,
atol=1e-6)) else mu
222
f0 = np.asarray(freq0, float).ravel()
223
f_even = np.linspace(f0.min(), f0.max(), len(f0))
224
ws = np.asarray(ws0, float)
225
if ws.shape[0] != f0.size: ws = ws.T
226
ws_i = np.empty((len(f_even), ws.shape[1]), float)
227
for k in range(ws.shape[1]):
228
ws_i[:, k] = np.interp(f_even, f0, ws[:, k], left=0.0, right=0.0)
229
freq_RAO = ds_rao["freq"].values; beta_RAO = ds_rao["beta"].values
230
Re = ds_rao[f"{dof}_re"].values; Im = ds_rao[f"{dof}_im"].values
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
163
==PAGE:170==
231
H = np.empty((len(f_even), len(beta_RAO)), complex)
232
for b in range(len(beta_RAO)):
233
tr = np.interp(f_even, freq_RAO , Re[b, :], left=0.0, right=0.0)
234
ti = np.interp(f_even, freq_RAO , Im[b, :], left=0.0, right=0.0)
235
H[:, b] = tr + 1j*ti
236
return f_even, mu, ws_i, beta_RAO , H

def preparing_Jensen_inputs_for_netsse(freq0, mu_deg , ws0, U_ms, psi_deg ,
ship):
239
mu = (np.asarray(mu_deg, float).ravel()) % 360
240
if mu.size >=2 and np.isclose((mu[-1]-mu[0])%360.0, 0.0, atol=1e-6): mu =
mu[:-1]
241
idx = np.argsort(mu); mu_safe = mu[idx]
242
f0 = np.asarray(freq0, float).ravel()
243
f_even = np.linspace(f0.min(), f0.max(), len(f0))
244
ws = np.asarray(ws0, float)[:, idx]
245
ws_i = np.empty((len(f_even), ws.shape[1]), float)
246
for k in range(ws.shape[1]):
247
ws_i[:, k] = np.interp(f_even, f0, ws[:, k], left=0.0, right=0.0)
248
omega = 2*np.pi*f_even
249
Hh, Hp, Hr, beta = Jensen_transfer_functions_2D(mu_safe , omega, U_ms,
ship, psi_deg)
250
beta = np.mod(beta, 360.0); ib = np.argsort(beta)
251
return f_even, mu_safe , ws_i, beta[ib], Hh[:, ib], Hp[:, ib], Hr[:, ib]

def buffer(beta_sorted , H_sorted , eps=1e-6):
254

255

257
H_buf

[-1]]], axis=1)

return beta_buf , H_buf

def align_to_grid(f_src, S_src, f_tgt):
264
"""Interpolate real PSD S_src from f_src onto f_tgt (outside range -> 0)
."""

# -------- Metrics & stats (ACCEL domain)
------------------------------------

271
def niae_band_acc(f_Hz, est_psd , gt_psd , f_cut):
272
f = np.asarray(f_Hz, float).ravel()
273
e = np.asarray(est_psd , float).ravel()
274
g = np.asarray(gt_psd,
float).ravel()
275
mask = np.ones_like(f, bool) if f_cut is None else (f <= float(f_cut))
276
if not np.any(mask): return np.nan
277
f, e, g = f[mask], e[mask], g[mask]
278
num = np.trapezoid(np.abs(e - g), f)
279
den = np.trapezoid(np.maximum(g, 0.0), f)
280
return np.nan if den <= 0 else (num/den)
281
282
def moments_acc(f, S):
283
m0 = np.trapezoid(S, f)
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
164
==PAGE:171==
284
m1 = np.trapezoid(f * S, f)
285
m2 = np.trapezoid((f**2) * S, f)
286
return m0, m1, m2
287

# ---- cubic-fit Tp finder (same as GT scans) ----
289
def tp_from_psd(f, S, smooth_Tp=True):
290
f = np.asarray(f, float).ravel()
291
S = np.asarray(S, float).ravel()
292
if f.size == 0: return np.nan
293
Ssafe = np.where(np.isfinite(S), S, -np.inf)
294
I = int(np.argmax(Ssafe))
295
if (not smooth_Tp) or I < 2 or I > f.size -3:
296
fp = f[I]
297
return 1.0/fp if (np.isfinite(fp) and fp > 0) else np.nan
298
x = f[I-2:I+3].astype(float); y = S[I-2:I+3].astype(float)
299
if not (np.all(np.isfinite(x)) and np.all(np.isfinite(y))):
300
fp = f[I]; return 1.0/fp if (np.isfinite(fp) and fp > 0) else np.nan
301
z = np.polyfit(x, y, 3); p = np.poly1d(z)
302
xd = np.linspace(x[0], x[-1], 200); yd = p(xd)
303
fp = float(xd[int(np.argmax(yd))])
304
return 1.0/fp if (np.isfinite(fp) and fp > 0) else np.nan
305
306
def stats_from_acc_psd(f, S):
307
if S.size == 0: return (np.nan,)*4
308
m0, m1, m2 = moments_acc(f, S)
309
Hm0_2 = 2.0*np.sqrt(max(m0, 0.0)) if np.isfinite(m0) else np.nan
310
Tz
= np.sqrt(m0/m2) if (m2 > 0 and np.isfinite(m0) and np.isfinite(m2
)) else np.nan
311
Tm01
= (m0/m1)
if (m1 > 0 and np.isfinite(m0) and np.isfinite(m1
)) else np.nan
312
Tp
= tp_from_psd(f, S, smooth_Tp=True)
313
return Hm0_2, Tz, Tm01, Tp

# Mean and Q90 lines

def mean_q90_lines(arr, color, tag):
352
if arr.size == 0: return
353
m
= float(np.mean(arr))
354

color=color, linestyle="-",
linewidth=1.0,
label=f"{tag} mean={m:.3g}")
356

label=f"{tag} Q90={q90:.3g}")

def scatter_with_identity(ax, x, y, panel_title , xlabel , ylabel , xlim):
384
try: ax.set_box_aspect(1)
385
except Exception: pass
386
lo, hi = xlim
387
ax.scatter(x, y, s=10, alpha=0.6)
388
ax.plot([lo, hi], [lo, hi], "--", linewidth=1)
389
ax.set_xlim(lo, hi); ax.set_ylim(lo, hi)
390
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
391
ax.set_title(panel_title)
392
ax.set_xlabel(xlabel); ax.set_ylabel(ylabel)
393
ax.grid(alpha=0.2)
394
395
lines = []
396
if x.size >= 2 and np.nanstd(x) > 0 and np.nanstd(y) > 0:
397
m, b = np.polyfit(x, y, 1)
398
r = np.corrcoef(x, y)[0, 1]
399
r2 = r*r if np.isfinite(r) else np.nan
Analysis of In-Service Data to Enhance Containership Response Estimation
166
==PAGE:173==
400
rmse = np.sqrt(np.mean((y - x)**2))
401
mae
= np.mean(np.abs(y - x))
402
ax.plot([lo, hi], [m*lo+b, m*hi+b], linewidth=1)
403
lines = [f"y = {m:.3g} x + {b:.3g}", f"R² = {r2:.3g}", f"RMSE = {
rmse:.3g}", f"MAE = {mae:.3g}", f"n = {x.size}"]
404
else:
405
lines = ["insufficient data"]
406
407
bbox = dict(boxstyle="round,pad=0.3", facecolor="white", edgecolor="
black", linewidth=0.8, alpha=0.9)
408
ax.text(0.98, 0.98, "\n".join(lines), transform=ax.transAxes , ha="right"
, va="top", fontsize=9, bbox=bbox)

def corr_grid_per_stat(stat_key , stat_pack , units_map , out_png ,
expected_limits=None, figsize=(8,10), pad_frac=0.05):
411
pretty = {"Hm0_2": "Hm0/2", "Tm": "Tm01", "Tz": "Tz", "Tp": "Tp"}
412
label = pretty.get(stat_key , stat_key)
413
414
fig, axes = plt.subplots(3, 2, figsize=figsize , constrained_layout=True)
415
for r, (name, dof) in enumerate([("Heave","heave"), ("Pitch","pitch"), (
"Roll","roll")]):
416
gt = stat_pack[dof]["gt"]; A = stat_pack[dof]["A"]; B = stat_pack[
dof]["B"]
417
keep = np.isfinite(gt) & np.isfinite(A) & np.isfinite(B)
418
if expected_limits is not None:
419
lims = expected_limits.get(label, {}).get(dof, {})
420
lo, hi = lims.get("min", None), lims.get("max", None)
421
if lo is not None: keep &= (gt >= lo) & (A >= lo) & (B >= lo)
422
if hi is not None: keep &= (gt <= hi) & (A <= hi) & (B <= hi)
423
x, yA, yB = gt[keep], A[keep], B[keep]
424
425
vals = np.concatenate([x, yA, yB]) if (x.size and yA.size and yB.
size) else np.array([])
426
if vals.size == 0:
427
xlim = (0.0, 1.0)
428
else:
429
lo_v, hi_v = float(np.nanmin(vals)), float(np.nanmax(vals))
430
span = hi_v - lo_v
431
pad = pad_frac*span if span > 0 else 0.5
432
xlim = (lo_v - pad, hi_v + pad)
433
434
unit = units_map.get(dof, "")
435
unit_str = f" [{unit}]" if unit else ""
436
437
scatter_with_identity(
438
axes[r, 0], x, yA,
439
panel_title=f"{name}: Case A vs GT",
440
xlabel=f"{label} (GT){unit_str}",
441
ylabel=f"{label} (Case A){unit_str}",
442
xlim=xlim
443
)
444
scatter_with_identity(
445
axes[r, 1], x, yB,
446
panel_title=f"{name}: Case B vs GT",
447
xlabel=f"{label} (GT){unit_str}",
448
ylabel=f"{label} (Case B){unit_str}",
449
xlim=xlim
450
)
451
452
fig.savefig(out_png , dpi=DPI); plt.close(fig)
453
print(f"saved: {out_png}")
454
